Preface 
This master thesis is submitted as a partial fulfillment for the degree of Master of Science in Architectural 
Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The thesis accounts for a total of 30 ECTS and 
has been conducted over a six-month period from the 23rd of January 2023 to the 23rd of June 2023.  
I would like to thank my supervisor Kristoffer Negendahl for his commitment, significant assistance, and 
continuous guidance throughout this project.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Bastian A. Zachriassen, 23rd June 2023 
 
 
 
==PAGE:5==
 
 
Abstract 
The construction industry’s contribution to CO2-emissions has sparked growing research and 
exploration on sustainable practices. This Master thesis examines the concepts of flexible buildings 
within the building sector. Flexible design allows buildings and building components to adapt to future 
changes, extending their lifespan thus optimising the use of resources. This approach offers the 
potential to reduce CO2-emissions throughout the full lifecycle of a building. While the concept of 
flexibility is gaining attention, there is still limited research on measuring and quantifying flexibility. The 
aim is to develop a method(s) for measuring flexibility, specifically of a load-bearing structure.  
Moreover, this study addresses the objective by developing a range of composite slab structures, with 
a particular emphasis on the application of Cross Laminated Timber (CLT) as the main material. CLT, 
renowned for its strength, durability, and environmental benefits, has gained recognition in recent years 
as a versatile construction element. The developed designs combine CLT with a range of various truss 
systems, to create structurally high-performing slab structures. Developed design solutions serve as 
specimens for measuring flexibility.   
Through an in-depth analysis of the structural performance of the developed designs, this research 
examines their potential as flexible load-bearing slab structures. The findings of the study provide insight 
into the measurement of flexibility and the utilisation of CLT in composite truss systems.

1. Introduction 
The building and construction industry accounted for up to 39% of energy and process-related CO2-
emissions in 2018. This significant contribution to emissions has raised concerns about its sustainability 
and environmental impacts, and addressing this challenge has become a pressing priority across the 
whole industry (Global Alliance for Buildings and Construction, 2019).   
In recent years, a growing recognition of the possibilities and potentials within flexible design of buildings 
has sparked a series of initiatives and research. The concept of flexible design focuses on creating 
buildings and building components that can adapt and evolve over time, thereby reducing the need for 
resource-intensive refurbishment and demolitions. Flexible design can extend the lifespan of a building 
or a building component, reduce use of resources and secure more optimal use of floor area 
(Negendahl, et al., 2023). 
Flexible designs embrace the adaptability of both the overall structure and the individual building 
components, thus allowing for modifications, expansions, and other future changes without 
compromising the structural integrity. This approach to structural design holds promises for achieving 
reductions in CO2-emissions throughout the lifecycle of a building. A study has shown that increasing 
the lifespan of a building from 50 to 100 or 150 years can result in theoretical reductions during its whole 
life-cycle, including embodied energy being calculated at 16% after 100 years 29% after 150 years 
(Andersen & Negendahl, 2023).  
Being able to measure the flexibility of a building or a building component could enable stakeholders 
within the building industry to make well-founded decisions in the design process towards more 
adaptable projects. While a high adaptability of a building or a building component generally is desired, 
the process of measuring this level of flexibility is a rather new topic. A few studies have investigated 
this subject, one which led to a proposal on implementation of Measurable Flexibility into the Danish 
building regulations (Negendahl, et al., 2023).  
While flexibility is a hot topic within sustainable development of the building industry, so is the use of 
timber for load-bearing structures. Timber is characterised by its high strength, durability, and 
environmental benefits. Utilising timber in construction is no new trend and has been applied in 
construction for thousands of years. However, due to the urgent need for sustainable alternatives to 
conventional materials such as concrete and steel, different timber structures has become increasingly 
popular (Det Europæiske Økonomiske og Sociale Udvalg, 2023).  
Cross Laminated Timber, or CLT, is product of the research and development within the production of 
timber structures and alternatives to more carbon-heavy concrete elements (Swedish Wood, 2019). Its 
cross-laminated lamella build-up induces a high strength and stability, enabling panels to resist large 
external forces. Combined with a versatility for manufacturing dimensions and shapes to suit different 
architectural and structural requirements, CLT is a relevant structure to include in the investigation of 
method(s) for measuring flexibility of a load-bearing structure.  
Since CLT was introduced in the late 90’s, different applications of the structure have been employed 
in manifold building projects. The possibilities of combining CLT with other materials or structural 
members makes it a versatile building component, of which its potentials as a flexible load-bearing slab 
structure are investigated in this study, by combining it with various truss systems.  
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:11==
2 
 
1.1 Project objectives  
This study aims to investigate the topic of measuring the flexibility of a load-bearing structure. The 
objective is explored by performing a literature review on current research, to account for state-of-the-
art theories within the subject. Additionally, a review of the structural properties and design methods of 
CLT is examined. Subsequently to this, a disclosure of existing innovative slab structures is presented.  
The objective is scrutinised by designing a range of composite slab structures utilising both Cross 
Laminated Timber and a combination of either Glued Laminated timber (GL) or steel profiles as truss 
members. The design process itself is executed by employing the parametric tool Rhino Grasshopper 
and appertaining plug-ins, wherein different geometric concepts are developed. A range of Key 
Performance Indicators (KPI) are defined to describe and measure the performance of the developed 
design solutions, which also facilitates the assessment and measurement of their flexibility.  
To further investigate and highlight the performance of the developed design solutions, an analysis of 
their structural performance is executed. The objective of this analysis is to individually compare across 
design solutions and point out the most optimal design solutions.  
Generally, the objectives of this study can be formulated in three short bullet points: 
- 
Develop a range of composite slab designs of CLT and either GL or steel profiles using a 
parametric approach. 
 
- 
Examine the flexibility of the developed design solutions utilising state-of-the-art methods for 
measuring flexibility. If available methods are inadequate, alternative methods should be 
proposed.  
 
- 
Analyse and assess the structural performance of the developed design solutions by applying 
Key Performance Indicators to highlight the best performing designs.  
1.1.1 Scope and limitations 
This study seeks to design a composite slab of CLT and either GL or steel profiles which can operate 
as a floor separating structure. The developed design solutions are assessed and measured for both 
their flexibility and structural performance, with respect to a range of pre-defined KPI’s.  
 
Due to limited time, not all relevant structural investigations of both the CLT, GL and steel profiles are 
conducted. The range of KPI’s are defined on behalf of an assessment of their relevance of inclusion, 
and additional verifications either recommended by code or producers are neglected in this project.  
 
The focus on structural calculations revolves around the CLT. Only tensile and compressive stresses 
are investigated for the GL and steel profiles, other relevant structural calculations are neglected.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:12==
3 
 
2. Methodology  
This chapter presents an outline of the different methods applied to investigate and meet the project 
objectives.  
 
2.1 Theoretical framework 
A literature review is executed to establish a theoretical framework. This framework functions as the 
main basis for investigating methods for measuring flexibility of buildings and building components. 
Furthermore, it provides the foundation for performing the required structural calculations of the 
developed design solutions. The literature review is divided into three different sub-sections, which each 
examines and outlines the methods and theories applied to meet the project objectives: 
 
1. A review of existing research within the topic of flexibility of a building or a building component.  
 
2. A review of existing floor structures which show design concepts or characteristics which are 
of relevance to the development of the proposed design solutions. These structures act as 
inspiration in the design process of the slabs. 
 
3. A revision of the current state-of-the-art methods to perform structural calculations of Cross 
Laminated Timber.  
 
2.2 Calibration of parametric script 
A calibration of the script developed in Karamba3D is executed, to ensure accurate structural 
calculations of the applied CLT. The calibration is conducted by utilising and comparing results across 
both Karamba3D, Strusoft FEM-design, and KLH Bemessung, both software which includes CLT in 
their material library. Two KLH ® CLT panels are chosen for an analysis and comparison of stresses 
and deformations across both ULS, SLS, and ALS design scenarios.  
 
2.3 Simulations 
Simulations of the design solutions is facilitated in Rhino Grasshopper utilising the calibrated script. A 
Monte Carlo method for a randomised approach of selecting combinations of input variables is applied 
to reduce the number of simulations.  
 
2.4 Sorting of data 
Both input variables and output results are exported from Rhino Grasshopper to Microsoft Excel. Three 
contiguous rounds of sorting and reducing the amount of design solutions are executed, to ensure that 
remaining designs meet defined criterion.  
 
2.5 Analysis 
Two separate analyses are conducted. Firstly, an in-depth analysis of the structural performance of the 
design solutions is conducted. The analysis focuses on the Key Performance Indicators and the 
interconnection between these. A specific spotlight on how the mass of the designs is reduced while 
still adhering to the structural criterion is kept. A few optimal designs are highlighted and presented.  
 
Additionally, an analysis of the design solutions flexibility is executed, by applying the proposed 
methods for measuring flexibility from the theoretical framework. A range of designs are chosen due to 
their structural performance and compared between their KPI’s. Both analyses enable to highlight 
several design solutions which performs well both within the structural behaviour and the flexibility.  
==PAGE:13==
4 
 
 
2.6 Recommendations 
Based on the theoretical framework and the analysis of flexibility, a recommendation for a step-by-step 
guide on measuring flexibility is presented. The guide is composed as a universal guide for the 
measurement of flexibility of any load-bearing structure and is not restricted to slab structures only.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:14==
5 
 
3. Theoretical framework 
This chapter presents a review of relevant research, literature, and existing knowledge on the 
investigated topics of flexible buildings, innovative slab design solutions, and Cross Laminated Timber. 
The review provides a foundation for proposed methods for measuring flexibility, the development of 
parametric scripts for structural calculations of CLT, and the analysis and discussion of the developed 
slab design solutions.  
 
3.1 Flexibility in construction 
Creating flexible architecture and adaptable buildings can play a role in reducing carbon emissions 
within the building industry and effectively contribute to achieving the Paris-agreement on reducing 
emissions by 40% before 2030 (Negendahl, et al., 2023). This makes the subject of flexible buildings 
vital to further develop to reinforce and extend the sustainable initiatives already made within the 
construction industry.  
 
3.1.1 History of flexibility  
The topic of flexibility itself is no new term to the industry and was already investigated by the Dutch 
architect N. John Habraken in 1960’s. Habraken developed the concept of Open Building in his book 
Supports: Alternative to Mass Housing from 1972 (English version of the original Dutch version from 
1962), where he proposed a framework for designing buildings and communities that can evolve and 
respond to changing user needs over time. Habraken distinguishes between two fundamentals of a 
building: “the support” and the “infill”. The support layer includes the main structural elements, such as 
foundations, vertical and horizontal load-bearing elements, and the primary service infrastructure 
(Habraken, 1972).  The infill refers to non-structural elements, which are easily modifiable and typically 
changes over relatively short time periods. Such layers are typically related to basic functions and 
aesthetics, and could involve partitions, floorings, fixtures, and even furniture. Habraken elaborates on 
the importance of a long lifespan for such support building components, as these should last decades 
and outlive the infill. Their ability to adapt should thus be met by incorporating a flexible design 
procedure (Habraken, 1972).  
 
Generally, Habraken’s Open Building Theory advocates for a design process which incorporates 
predictions of changing needs and uses thus, to build houses which can accommodate such changes. 
This built-in flexibility will provide adaptability, provoking more sustainable, responsive, and cost-
efficient buildings.  
 
3.1.2 Shearing Layers of Change Theory 
Flexibility of buildings is often discussed with a holistic approach and the literature on the topic is 
extensive. Yet, research and studies on flexible load-bearing structures are limited. In 1994 Stewart 
Brand elaborated on The Shearing Layers Theory, a theory originally introduced by Frank Duffy, which 
provides a hypothesis on how different layers of a building change over time and how such changes 
can be obliged (Brand, 1994).   
 
==PAGE:15==
6 
 
 
 
Figure 1: Shearing Layers of Change by Stewart Brand (Openbuilding.co, 2023) 
 
Shearing’s Theory divides a building into five layers, each having their own purpose, lifecycle, and thus 
lifespan. In Brand’s presentation of the theory, the Structure depicts the members which carry and 
transport both vertical loads and induces stability by being able to resist horizontal loads. In his book 
How buildings learn: What happens after they’re built Brand presents a design imperative stating that 
“An adaptive building has to allow slippage between the differently-pased systems of Site, Structure, 
Skin, Services, Space Plan, and Stuff. Otherwise the slow systems block the flow of the quick ones, 
and the quick ones tear up the slow ones with their constant change” (Brand, 1994). Generally, Brand 
states that the Shearing Layers must act as one, yet still be able to be separated over the lifespan of 
the building. This way of creating a building enables circular design choices early in the design process. 
 
The Shearing Layer Theory is utilised as a foundational concept for this study, which indeed does focus 
on the development a new load-bearing slab structure. As the theory considers the Structure as the 
main frame, it must be able to accommodate future changes of Space plan, Stuff etc. thus, it is crucial 
to investigate how to design a versatile slab structure, which can comply with such changes.  
 
3.1.3 Refurbishment of existing building stock 
The development of new buildings is often more carbon heavier than refurbishment of existing buildings. 
One study has shown that emissions related to development of new housing can be emit up to 50x 
more CO2 compared to an extensive refurbishment of the existing building (Regerings 
klimapartnerskaber - Bygge- og anlægssektoren, 2019). The research project highlights a case study 
which investigates an extensive refurbishment of multi-story residentials originally erected in the 1970’s. 
Only the existing load-bearing structure was preserved, while additional existing materials were 
replaced. The renovation showed a CO2-footprint of 29 tons while its alternative solution of demolishing 
and erecting a new, similar housing project had an estimated CO2-emission of 1625 tons.  
 
The possibilities of refurbishment of existing building stock, rather than creating new buildings increases 
as the flexibility of the building’s components increase. This statement is supported by Habraken’s and 
Brand’s proposals for creating a support or structure layer, which can comply with the future changes 
and thus contribute to the chance of avoiding construction of new CO2 and cost-heavy building projects.  
 
3.1.4 Flexibility and lifespan 
With the Open Building Theory and The Shearing Layer Theory as reference points, a hypothesis on 
the relationship between flexibility and the lifespan of a building load-bearing structure is proposed. The 
hypothesis states that as the flexibility of a building component increases so does its lifespan. While no 
==PAGE:16==
7 
 
data on the statement is provided, the reasoning for this hypothesis is built upon an assumption, that 
the more flexible a structure is the better it can accommodate future changes. This hypothesis forms 
the foundation for the objective of developing flexible design solutions incorporates a long lifespan as a 
design feature.  
 
 
Figure 2: Relationship between the flexibility and lifespan of a load-bearing structure 
 
An example of how flexibility can be integrated early in the design process is House for Artists by 
Apparata Architects. The project involves a residential building where a simple block design gives 
flexibility to future structural alterations. A plan drawing of the project is seen in Figure 3. While House 
of Artists makes use of interconnecting doors which facilitate spatial openings, a similar design could 
be composed by enabling uncomplicated alterations or removal of load-bearing elements.  
 
 
Figure 3: House for Artists by Apparata Architects (Pelsmakers & Warwick, 2022) 
 
3.1.5 Measurable flexibility 
One of the objectives of this study is to measure and quantify the flexibility of slab design solutions. To 
do so, a classification system by K. Negendahl, F. Carlucci and F. Fiorito is utilised. It exhibits six 
different flexibility classes whereas Class 1 represents the most flexible solution and Class 6 the least. 
Its purpose is to visualise the distribution of the investigated results around the zero. The index system 
is developed to classify Normalised Energy Consumption Values (NECV), a parameter which quantifies 
Lifespan
Flexibility
==PAGE:17==
8 
 
the energy consumption of a building during different scenarios. As this study investigates load-bearing 
structures, the method is adjusted to fit current objective. The fundamentals of calculating NECV are 
briefly described, and then the adjusted method utilised in this study is presented.  
 
 
Figure 4: Flexibility index classification system (Carlucci, Fioritio, & Negendahl, 2023)

energy consumption values are transformed to represent the Key Performance Indicator (KPI) results 
from the simulated design solutions: 𝐸𝐶𝑘,𝑆𝑖= 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘,𝑆𝑖 and 𝐸𝐶𝑘,𝐶𝑆= 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘,𝐶𝑆, whereas 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘,𝐶𝑆 is the KPI

certain performance of the investigated design, such as a stress, strain, or any other relevant structural 
behaviour. The weighting factor w is excluded, due to a lack of a reference structure in this study.

Whilst Equation 3.2 is unitless due to it being normalised, a simplified method of plotting the KPI results 
for different scenarios is also proposed.  This approach allows to compare the baseline and the future 
scenario, while the units of the KPI is preserved. Effectively, this means that the baseline result is 
transformed to zero and function as a point of reference for remaining results:  
 
𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑛𝐶𝑆= 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝐶𝑆−𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝐶𝑆

3.4 
 
Measuring flexibility of multiple designs  
In some cases, the designer will have to measure the flexibility of multiple design solutions at once. 
This could be due to an objective of finding the most flexible solution within a range of different design 
solutions. This is also the case in this report. Due to the shear amount of developed design solutions 
this analysis can be comprehensive and time-consuming.  
 
A method to quickly assess the flexibility of multiple design solutions at once is proposed. Its concept 
is to plot the median against the IQR for every KPI result within each design solution, thus again utilising 
a graphical approach on the assessment of the flexibility. IQR is calculated by subtracting the 1st quartile 
(25%-point) from the 3rd quartile (75%-point) of the results, as seen in Equation  3.5.  
 
𝐼𝑄𝑅= 𝑄3 −𝑄1 
3.5 
 
The calculation of IQR should be executed after applying Equation 3.3 and 3.4. This is very important, 
to make sure that the baseline result has been transformed to zero and thus works as a point of 
reference for remaining results.  
 
The assessment approach of the plot is based on a hypothesis, which states that the lower the median 
and the IQR, the more flexible the design solution is. This hypothesis is only valid for KPI’s which 
performance increases as their magnitude decreases, which is also the case for all KPI’s in this study. 
The chain of reasoning behind this hypothesis is, that a “low” median indicates that at least 50% of the 
KPI results is more receptive to future alterations, compared to if the median were “high”. As the IQR 
describes the range between the 1st and 3rd quartile, it portrays the distribution of data above and below 
the median. Thus, it describes how 50% of the KPI’s reacts when subjected to the different scenarios.  
Effectively, a combination of a low median with a low IQR illustrates a design which is more receptive 
to the effects of the future scenarios, thus making it more flexible.  
 
To demonstrate the hypothesis a fictive example is presented. It depicts KPI results for two different 
designs subjected to a total of six different combinations of future scenarios. The results could resemble 
stresses or deformations etc. Design 1 exhibits a flexible solution. Both its median and IQR is “low” 
compared to Design 2 which illustrates a less flexible example. When a limit value such as a max. 
allowed stress or deformation is introduced in the figure (dashed line), it is intuitive to the designer that 
Design 1 is more flexible since its median in further from exceeding limit. Thereby, it can resist more 
future alterations as it is less effected by such compared to Design 2. 
 
 
Figure 5: Results and plots of Design 1 and Design 2.  
 
==PAGE:19==
10 
 
3.2 Cross Laminated Timber 
Cross Laminated Timber, or commonly referred to as CLT, are minimum 3 layers of planks of timber 
glued together at right angles. This build-up creates a two-dimensional massive timber panel, which 
can carry large both in- and out-of-plane forces (Koppelhuber, Pock, & Wallner-Novak, 2013). CLT is a 
versatile building component and can be applied in a broad range of different building projects. Its 
composition enables it to be utilised as walls, slabs, roofs, and even slim members such as beams or 
columns. Due to timbers ductility, CLT can be produced in various shapes providing the designer with 
a high degree of architectural freedom.  
 
 
Figure 6: Designation of parts in Cross Laminated Timber panel (Koppelhuber, Pock, & Wallner-Novak, 2013) 
 
CLT was developed at Austrian universities in the 1990’s and the production of the materials has been 
growing for the past years (CBI - Ministry of Forein Affairs, 2017). The material is characterised by its 
high level of pre-fabrication, which allows for an optimised building process on site. Moreover, CLT 
members can be internally joined by nailing or screws, which enables for connections which refers to a 
design-for-disassembly concept (Swedish Wood, 2019).  
 
3.2.1 CLT and environmental challenges 
As environmental concerns with regards to greenhouse gas emissions grow, the need for sustainable 
practices and building components also grow. As a sustainable and renewable material, timber has 
gained attention as a viable alternative to conventional construction materials with higher CO2-
footprints. CLT is specifically considered a CO2-efficient alternative to carbon-heavy concrete elements, 
which has been widely used especially within the Danish building sector since the 1980’s (Pedersen, 
Dyck-Madsen, & Jarby, 2021). This makes it a significant material to utilise, on the journey towards a 
reduction of embodied carbon in the construction industry.  
 
Figure 7 presents a graph of six different ways of constructing a four-story building. It shows the CO2-
equivalents for the production phase, from raw material and to the delivery of the finished product on 
site. A significant difference in CO2-emissions is seen for the concrete frame, compared to the five 
alternatives designed with a load-bearing structures of timber. The difference in CO2-equivalents is 
close to 100
𝑘𝑔
𝑚2, which for an apartment of 100 m2 is a dispersion of 10 tons of carbon emission (Swedish 
Wood, 2019). Such studies, underlines the potentials to reduce the GHG-emissions of buildings and 
supports the choice of CLT being the main structural material applied in this study due to its both 
structural and environmental properties.  
 
==PAGE:20==
11 
 
 
Figure 7: Greenhouse gas emissions (CO2-equivalents) from the production phase for six different designs of a 
four-story building (Swedish Wood, 2019).  
 
3.3 Structural design of Cross Laminated Timber 
Regardless of being invented in the 1990’s, to this day CLT still remains to be included in the Eurocodes. 
Since no official regulations exist, this thesis will employ state-of-the-art literature and knowledge on 
CLT’s mechanical properties and structural performance. Such techniques on CLT origins from both 
producers of CLT, research studies on the material, and from engineering related publications on the 
topic. As CLT to some extent has mechanical properties similar glued laminated timber, which indeed 
is included in the Eurocodes, some considerations related to structural calculations of glulam are 
implemented on CLT calculations also.   
 
The following sections outlines the theoretical framework, which forms the basis of the structural 
calculations conducted of CLT in this study. 
 
3.3.1 Partial safety factors 
When performing structural design of timber structures, the duration of the subjected loads is of 
importance to the characteristic strengths of the material. To account for the impact of load durations, 
loads are assigned a load duration factor 𝑘𝑚𝑜𝑑 dependent on its duration (Dansk Standard, 2007). Since 
CLT is yet not included in the Eurocodes, the 𝑘𝑚𝑜𝑑 value for GL is utilised:

3.3.6 Design in serviceability limit state 
When performing verifications of the serviceability limit state of a slab it generally involves deformations, 
vibrations and sagging of the element. For this study, only deflections are considered while vibrations 
and appertaining SLS calculations are neglected.  
 
Shear correction coefficient 
Due to CLT's cross-layered structure, layers placed in transverse direction to the span direction 
experience what is generally referred to as rolling shear, when subjected to out-of-plane loading. Rolling 
shear induces large shear deformations as cross-layers rolling shear strength 𝑓𝑣,𝑟 is substantially lower 
than of the longitudinal layers. Several methods can be applied to determine the effective shear 
capacity, including but not limited to the Gamma Method according to Schelling, Kreuzinger’s Shear 
==PAGE:26==
17 
 
Analogy Method, and the flexible-beam theory according to Timoshenko (Wallner-Novak, Augustin, 
Koppelhuber, & Pock, 2018).  
 
Since this report will not dive into differentiations between methods, a summary of the basic principles 
of the flexible-beam theory by Timoshenko is given. Furthermore, when conducting two-dimensional 
structural calculations of out-of-plane loading, Timoshenkos theory will be applied to account for shear 
deformations by introduction of a shear correction factor 𝜅.  
 
Timoshenko’s Beam Theory 
Timoshenkos Beam Theory considers the contribution from shear to the deformations and the 
compliance of cross-layers. A shear correction, 𝜅= 𝜅𝑥= 𝜅𝑦, defines the modification of shear modulus, 
G, as seen in Equation 3. (Petersen, 2020):

3.3.7 Structural fire design 
Structural fire safety design is a crucial process of the design of timber structures, conducted to verify 
that the structure can withstand its load-bearing capacity during a designated fire situation. The type of 
fire can be calculated either as a standard fire or a parametric fire case (Petersen, 2020). This study 
utilises a standard fire situation. While no structural fire safety design in ALS is conducted for the 
simulations of the developed design solutions, it is included in the calibration of the parametric script to 
ensure accurate calculations of the CLT.  
 
Loading conditions 
When verifying structural elements for a fire load case, following load combination as prescribed by 
DS/EN 1990 (Dansk Standard, 2007): 
 
𝐺𝑘+ 𝜓1,1𝑄𝑘,1 + ∑𝜓2,𝑖𝑄𝑘,𝑖
𝑛
𝑖=1
 
3.37 
  
Charring rate 
A non-treated timber surface exposed to radiant temperatures around 250° will ignite. Burning of the 
cross-section of the structure will continue inwards, however at a relatively constant speed making the 
burning rate overall predictable (Swedish Wood, 2019). The charring rate 𝛽0 of a KLH® CLT is 0,65 
𝑚𝑚
𝑚𝑖𝑛 
(European Technical Assessment, 2021).  
 
==PAGE:27==
18 
 
One-dimensional charring rate for a CLT element with edge-glued boards or with gaps between edge 
boards being ≤ 2 mm can be determined using following equation: 
 
𝑑𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟,0 = 𝛽0𝑇 
3.38 
  
Dependent on the type of adhesive (glue) used for bonding of the cross layers, two different charring 
sequences are possible: melamine-urea-formaldehyde/MUF adhesives with a constant, linear charring 
rate. Or polyurethane/PUR adhesives with a double charring rate at the first 25 mm of each board due 
to delamination. This study use MUF adhesives to avoid delamination (Swedish Wood, 2019).  
 
 
Figure 9: Reduced cross-section due to a fire situation 
 
Reduced cross-section 
As explained a fire situation will lead to a loss of cross-section depth 𝑑𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟,0. Furthermore, comparative 
calculations show a heavy reduction of the material properties of the layer located closest to charred 
section (Petersen, 2020). To account for this, a further thickness of 𝑑0 is deducted from the net cross-
section height. As proposed by Östman et al. a correlation between thickness and direction of the 
element (horizontal or vertical), fire protective cladding and axial force direction on fire exposed side 
can affect and potentially increase 𝑑0, however for this study 𝑑0 = 7 𝑚𝑚 is used (Östman, et al., 2010). 
The effective cross-section depth 𝑑𝑒𝑓 is then found as: 
 
𝑑𝑒𝑓= 𝑑𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟,0 + 𝑑0 
3.39 
 
EN-1995-1-2 prescribes a method of exploiting reduced cross-section for analysis under loading 
conditions as presented in Equation 3.37.  
 
3.3.8 Acoustic conditions 
According to the Danish regulations BR18 one must design buildings, so they have acoustic conditions 
which are satisfactory for health and comfort to their users. Such conditions are framed as functional 
requirements, which describes minimum conditions and considerations which must be met accordingly 
to the type of building (Bolig- og Planstyrelsen, 2023). Some of these provisions include airborne and 
impact sound insulation between rooms. Indeed, when designing room separating floor structures 
(slabs) the impact sound insulation of the structure is of great importance: the normalised impact sound 
value 𝐿𝑛,𝑤
′
 describes the sound absorption of the structure when subjected to vibrations from physical 
contact such as footsteps. For this study impact sound insulation of the investigated slabs is performed, 
while airborne sound insulation between rooms is excluded.  
 
DS/EN ISO 12354-2:2017 prescribes a method to calculate the equivalent weighted normalised impact 
sound pressure level 𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤 for a homogeneous floor construction (Dansk Standard, 2017):  
 
==PAGE:28==
19 
 
𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤= 164 −(35 lg
𝑚′
(1 𝑘𝑔/𝑚2 ))  𝑑𝐵 
3.40 
 
While Equation 3.40 prescribes a method for calculating 𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤, it is significant to stress that this 
formula is developed for application for homogenous concrete floors. Since this study employs a 
combination of CLT and either GL or CHS steel profiles as trusses, the developed design solutions in 
this study does not match the input criteria for utilising Equation 3.40 to determine 𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤. However, 
as this method is assessed to be a relatively simplified approach it is utilised, nonetheless.  
 
To apply the formula for this exact study, a simplification of the developed composite slab designs is 
made. As most of the developed designs does not incorporate a homogenous geometry due to their 
truss systems, this truss layers are homogenised into a solid layer. The mass of the truss is then equally 
distributed over the total area of the design solution. The principle is visualised Figure 10.  
 
 
Figure 10: Principle of homogenisation of cross-section  
 
3.4 Innovative slab structures 
In a building industry with a growing focus of the utilisation of renewable materials and their lifespan, 
the requirement for innovative slab structures has been fostering several ingenious designs. This 
section provides a short summary of a range of composite slab designs, which has been providing 
inspiration for the development of the proposed design solutions of this study. This could be due to their 
structural performance or other related architectural features. The presented solutions all utilise timber 
to maintain their structural integrity.  
 
3.4.1 Posi-Joist™ 
Posi-Joist™ by Palsgaard Spær is a timber-steel composite beam which utilises a simple truss 
structure. It can be applied as part of room dividing slab structures and in flat roof structures.  Its internal 
clearance between its upper and lower flanges provides a high flexibility for the placement of 
installations and enables for addition of insulation insertion (Palsgaard Spær, 2023).  
 
==PAGE:29==
20 
 
 
Figure 11: Posi-Joist™ by Palsgaard Spær (Palsgaard Spær, 2023) 
 
The end connection of Posi-Joist™ makes them versatile and flexible, as they can be connected to both 
GL beams, timber stud walls, concrete elements, or masonry walls.  
 
 
Figure 12: 3D-rendering of end connection of a Posi-Joist™ (Palsgaard Spær, 2023) 
 
The possibilities of addition of internal posts to transfer vertical loads to abutments at midspan, 
potentially does enable for future typology changes which could affect the statical system of the slab 
structure. Moreover, due to hollow geometry of Posi-Joist™ cross-bracing can be introduced to carry 
horizontal forces and provide global stability of the super structure.   
 
 
Figure 13: Abutment at midspan and cross-bracing of Posi-Joist™ beams (Palsgaard Spær, 2023) 
 
==PAGE:30==
21 
 
While the Posi-Joist™ offers a smart solution for the load-bearing part of a slab structure, it does not 
deliver a complete solution which function as a slab. Additional flooring must be installed to ensure a 
planar upper surface.  
 
3.4.2 KLH® Rib Elements 
KLH® Rib Elements provide a combination of GL ribs and CLT panels, which works a composite cross-
section. At spans above 6 meters the rib elements usually offer more effective solutions compared to 
alternatives such as a pure CLT panel, due to their effective stress and strain distribution between upper 
panel and lower rib. Allowing for long spans this slab solution provides designers the freedom of making 
open floor areas with possibilities of flexible room concepts. Furthermore, internal spacing between the 
ribs allow for addition of insulation or installations (KLH Massivhols GmbH, 2019).  
 
     
 
Figure 14: 3D-render of longitudinal connection of rib elements and connection to a CLT panel wall (KLH 
Massivhols GmbH, 2019) 
 
KLH® Rib Elements possess a high load-bearing capacity and the combination of rib and panel provides 
a large bending stiffness. Additionally, the elements can resist large shear stresses occurring at the 
supports, which are mainly absorbed by the shear strength of the rib itself. While the hollow space 
between ribs allows for routing of installations in parallel direction, perpendicular pipes, and ducts etc., 
must be traced above or below the cross-section of the rib elements. This flooring build-up can cause 
deep slab-structures which is unwanted for both architectural and economic reasons. Furthermore, this 
could counteract the possibilities of incorporating future changes due to a limited space, thus reducing 
flexibility of the slab.  
 
3.4.3 KLH® Timber concrete composites 
Timber and concrete composite structures (TTC) have been widely available within the construction 
industry for several decades. One design solution is the combination of KLH® CLT panels and an upper 
layer of concrete. This slab structure draws advantage from the characteristics of both types of 
materials. Tensile stresses are designed to occur in the CLT, which has a high tensile strength while 
compressive forces are obtained by the upper layer of concrete screed (KLH Massivholz GmbH, 2019).   
 
KLH ® TCC structures allow large spans due to an increased stiffness of the cross-section compared 
to pure CLT alternatives. Additionally, the concrete provides improved acoustic properties and a cross-
section which is less vulnerable to vibrate due to its high mass. 
 
The connection between the CLT and the concrete plays a vital role in the structural capacity of the 
structure, as these joints must carry excessive shear forces. The CLT panels are produced with grooves 
to further strengthen the shear joints, while screws provide additional strength to resist longitudinal 
shear. The grooves can also carry some of the shear forces as compression, due to their incorporated 
layout.  
==PAGE:31==
22 
 
 
Figure 15: 3D-render of CLT and concrete composite floor structure (KLH Massivholz GmbH, 2019) 
 
Even though TCC’s are an auspicious alternative to carbon-heavy slab structures, TCC’s are obviously 
dependent on the use of concrete. Both the production, its related processes, and on-site handling of 
concrete emit substantial higher amounts of CO2 compared to the use timber. (Birgisdottir, Malmqvist, 
Potting, Nygaard Rasmussen, & Moncaster, 2018). While the incorporation of concrete brings both 
static and acoustic advantages, this demand for concrete can obstruct the development towards 
structures utilising renewable materials.  
 
3.4.4 Rematter ® floor slabs 
Rematter® floor slabs are constructed from a frame of solid timber beams which encircle a layer of 
rammed earth infill (Rematter, 2023). A 3-layered timber panel is installed on top to ensure a stiffening 
layer which also provides vibration resistance. The layer of rammed earth is fully circular and provides 
a shielding of the timber against fire. Additionally, it contributes to the thermal mass and the acoustic 
properties of the slab structure.   
 
Rematter® floor slabs can resist live loads up to 5
𝑘𝑁
𝑚2 and meets fire requirements up to REI90. When 
combining with certain flooring build-ups its impact sound insulation can reach ≤53 𝑑𝐵. These 
properties make it a versatile structure since these properties often are requirements for slabs when 
applied in multi-storey residentials or office buildings.  
 
 
Figure 16: 3D-render of Rematter ® floor slab. 1) is the 3-layered timber, 2) is the solid timber beams and 3) is 
the rammed earth (Rematter, 2023) 
 
While offering low CO2-footprints, Rematter ® does incorporate a non-renewable material with its 
inclusion of rammed earth infill. Such a feature might be CO2-efficient but require a use of a material 
which is somehow limited.  
 
==PAGE:32==
23 
 
 
4. Configuration of parametric script 
This chapter outlines the development of the parametric script in which all structural calculations of the 
developed design solutions are performed. The aim of this chapter is to calibrate, test and review the 
developed scripts to ensure accurate calculations of internal stresses and deformations.   
 
While one of the objectives of this study is to investigate numerous different design concepts and 
iterations, manifold simulations are conducted. To ease and optimise this simulative process, a range 
of parametric scripts for performing calculations of both stresses and deformations of the slab designs 
are developed. The scripts are composed in the interactive, parametric tool Rhinoceros 7 - 
Grasshopper, utilising the incorporated API FEA-plugin Karamba3D.   
 
Karamba3D offer a range of standard library building components, however Cross Laminated Timber 
is not included. Thus, to enable accurate structural calculations of the proposed design solutions, the 
script must be able to simulate a similar structural behaviour identical of how CLT behaves.  
 
The development, calibration, and testing of the script is executed by bilaterally operating two state-of-
the-art software for performing calculations of CLT: KLH Bemessung + KLHDesigner and Strusoft FEM-
Design. KLH Bemessung is chosen since it is provided by the manufacturer KLH Massivholz, whose 
KLH® CLT panels are utilised in this study. Strusoft FEM-Design is chosen because it is one of few 
FEA-software incorporating CLT in its catalogue of structures. More thorough descriptions of both 
software tools are outlined in forthcoming pages, which also presents three summaries of the pros- and 
cons of each of them.  
 
The Karamba3D script is developed utilising its standard components for performing Finite Element 
Analysis (FEA) on structures. As CLT is not included, it is cultivated in the software as an orthotropic 
shell structure with diverging material properties in its two different directions. The input material 
properties are sourced from Table 3.  
 
 
 
 
Figure 17: Mesh to Shell component in Karamba3D resembling a CLT panel 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:33==
24 
 
KLH Bemessung / KLHdesigner 
 
 
 
2D-verification tool based on ETA-06/0138. Split 
between two software: KLH Bemessung which 
performs 
cross 
sectional 
calculation 
and 
KLHdesigner which contains cross section data. 
 
User friendly 
KLH Bemessung needs few input parameters to 
calculate utilisations rates in both ULS, SLS and 
ALS design. Its interface is intuitive and 
applicable also for non-structural-oriented users. 
KLH Bemessung has a print-to-PDF function 
which provides a clear report of the structural 
design calculations. Cross-section data on 
analysed elements are manually imported from 
third-party software KLHdesigner, thus making 
the calculation process relatively slow and 
exposed to errors on selection of cross section 
properties.  
 
2D-calculations  
 
 
KLH Bemessung are based on 2D-calculations 
as prescribed in ETA-06/0138. SLS requirements 
are based on chosen national annexes, while 
frequency and acceleration criterions are based 
on EN 1995-1-1. For calculations of shear 
stresses and deformations the shear correction 
factor according to Timoshenko as described in 
Section 3.3.6.  
 
Semi-precise results 
The software is built as an Excel-file with macros, 
and intermediate results between input and 
output data is either limited or hidden to its user. 
However, most intermediate results often are 
dispensable to the user and all verifications 
principles can be found in ETA-06/0138. 
 
 
Limitations 
KLH Bemessung is limited to deck, roof, wall, and 
lintel calculations. Calculations within the deck 
category are restricted to simple geometries of 
rectangle elements, with no inclusion of potential 
recesses or non-evenly distributed loading. 
Furthermore, in-plane loading on deck elements 
are not selectable. KLH Bemessung does include 
tools for calculating rib-element structures in a 
combination of CLT and glulam but is limited to 
longitudinal directed glulam beams in span 
direction.  
 
==PAGE:34==
25 
 
Strusoft FEM design  
 
 
 
 
State-of-the-art FEA-software by StruSoft.  
 
 
 
 
Advanced FEA 
FEM design offers an advanced FEA-tool for 
calculation of CLT-elements. The Finite Element 
mesh is automatically created and optimised. Its 
applied mechanical model is based on “the 
equivalent single layer method (ESLM)”. The 
element stiffness matrix of this methods uses a 
homogenisation procedure where the same 
equation number remains by the final equation 
system, no matter the number of layers of the 
shell (StruSoft, 2023). With regards to laminated 
composite shells FEM-Design adopts the first-
order shear deformation theory, Reissner-Mindlin 
Theory (StruSoft, 2023). 
 
Application Program Interfaces (API) 
FEM 
Design 
supports 
API 
with 
Rhino 
Grasshopper 
and 
other 
software. 
The 
possibilities of combining FEA with a parametric 
tool such as Grasshopper make the working 
environment more flexible. The input data and 
initial setup for the analysis is kept in 
Grasshopper, imported into FEM Design where 
the analyses are run. Output data of results can 
be exported back to Grasshopper. Nonetheless, 
when performing manifold simulations this cross-
border software setup does have its limitations as 
FEM design analysis is time-consuming.  
 
Documentation module 
Like KLH Bemessung, FEM Design has an 
integrated documentation module. While the 
documentation itself is very clear, the process of 
applying the tool can be prolonged. When 
applied thoroughly, this documentation module 
can provide detailed, yet clear reports of cross-
section calculations, stresses, strains, and 
utilisation rates with regards to the chosen 
standards and National Annexes.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:35==
26 
 
Karamba3D 
 
 
Parametric FEA-plugin for Rhino Grasshopper.  
 
 
 
 
Parametric tool  
Karamba3D is embedded in the 3D-environment 
of Grasshopper. It is a parametric and interactive 
tool which can perform FEA-calculations. Since 
geometry 
is 
designed 
parametrically 
in 
Grasshopper or in Rhino, designs can rapidly be 
changed. Karamba3D catalogue of various 
structural components is far-reaching, however 
CLT panels are yet to be included.  
 
Orthotropic materials 
Since Karamba3D excludes CLT, the orthotropic 
stiffness properties of the panels must be 
manually established. While the stiffness of the 
panel depends on the properties of the individual 
layers, a calibrated model has been developed to 
semi-accurately depict stresses and strains in 
panels. Karamba3D includes shell elements, 
which are divided into meshes for the FEA. 
However, since CLT panels is excluded from its 
catalogue, the internal stresses of the panels 
must be manually calculated by means of 
theories outlined in Section 3.3. Cross section 
data is imported from KLHdesigner, to accurately 
depict the properties of KLH ® CLT panels.  
 
Instant results 
Karamba3D creates semi-instantaneous results 
of its FEA calculations, thus making its software 
suitable for manifold simulations. Combined with 
various methods of graphically visualising 
stresses and strains throughout structures it is 
adequate software for initial simulation iterations. 
 
Combined API 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Parameterised 
geometric 
models 
can 
be 
combined with countless other plug-ins, such as 
the 
optimisation 
algorithm 
Galapagos 
(Karamba3D, 
2023). 
The 
possibilities 
of 
combining 
Karamba3D 
with 
other 
Rhino 
applications makes the process of performing 
FEA in Karamba3D versatile and adaptive. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:36==

4.1 Testing of methods 
Although KLH Bemessung and FEM Design incorporate KLH®-panels in their catalogue of structural 
components, Karamba3D does not include them. Therefore, a Material and Element component 
resembling the material properties of CLT is generated in Karamba3D. To ensure a material behaviour 
corresponding to input properties of CLT in FEM design, two baseline scenarios as seen in Table 7 are 
defined, tested, and evaluated across the three software. Internal composite shell stresses are 
calculated according to theories outlined in Section 3.3. Cross-sectional properties of KLH® CLT- 
panels are imported from KLHdesigner to Karamba3D to accurately depict panel data. Through multiple 
iterations the Karamba3D model is calibrated to semi-accurately depict the structural behaviour of CLT 
in both FEM design and KLH Bemessung. Since it is considered out-of-scope of this thesis to develop 
a Karamba3D script to accurately resemble CLT, discrepancies of results across all software are 
accepted, however kept to an obtainable minimum. It is relevant to emphasise that FEM design and 
KLH Bemessung make use of different calculational methods, and divergence between results is to be 
expected. Two KLH®-panel types are used for testing: KLH® 3s 110mm TL and KLH® 5s 200mm TL. 
Each scenario is evaluated within five span intervals from 3.0 m. to 5.0 m. to acquire suitable data. 
Parameters as seen in Table 6 is evaluated and compared between the three software. Results of tests 
are outlined in 4.1.1 and discussed in 4.1.2. 
 
Since KLH Bemessung and FEM Design has integrated load combinations in their calculation 
procedure, an identical load combination is incorporated in the Karamba3D script to ensure similar load 
magnitudes. Load combinations are established as prescribed in DS/EN 1990 DK NA:2021 (Trafik-, 
Bygge-, og Boligstyrelsen, 2021) and summarized in Table 5.  
 
As seen in Table 5, the calibration of the Karamba3D scripts is executed for ALS, which incorporates 
design for structural fire loads. Thus, the charred and reduced cross-section theory as presented in 
Section 3.3.7 is applied. However, due to limitations of time the ALS calculations are only performed 
and incorporated in the development of the script and are neglected in the further analysis of the 
structural performance and flexibility of the developed design solutions. This is further discussed in 
Section 11.9.  
 
Table 5: Load combinations cf. DS/EN 1990 DK NA:2021. Note that certain load types are neglected, since these 
are not considered in the baseline scenario.

4.1.2 Discussion 
Generally, the testing of methods has been conducted to calibrate the Karamba3D script by ensuring 
that the resulting stresses and strains fall within acceptable variations of those found in FEM Design. In 
addition, KLH Bemessung results have been obtained and plotted to provide a comparable benchmark 
using 2D-calculational method results. As depicted in charts presented in section 4.1.1, there is a direct 
correlation between the increase in span length and the amplification of internal stresses or 
deformations. This relationship is expected across all evaluation parameters since the internal stresses 
and strains of slabs generally intensify with the span length increase. It is noteworthy to mention when 
analysing the results, that the simulations across software have been limited to include two panel types 
and certain span lengths. For a more exact calibration of the Karamba3D script, it would have been 
relevant to include more panel types e.g., a 7-layer panel or several different 3- and 5-layer types. 
Furthermore, larger varieties of span lengths could have been combined with said panel types.  
 
Figure 20 and Figure 22 visualise a slightly curved almost linear relationship between bending stresses 
and span length across all three software. For both slab types bending stresses computed in FEM 
Design lies below plots of KLH Bemessung and Karamba3D. For the 200 mm panel Karamba3D shows 
bending stresses marginally larger than KLH Bemessung results, while the opposite trend is seen for 
the 110 mm panel. These minor incoherences could be due to how the cross-sectional properties are 
calculated. As outlined in Equation 3.10, 3.11 and 3.12 the individual layer thickness affects the moment 
of inertia and the section modulus, since the thickness is found as 𝑡= ∑
𝑡1 + 𝑡𝑖+ ⋯ 
𝑛
𝑖=1
. While 
Karamba3D does not distinguish between layers and is modelled as one element with a thickness of 
𝑡= ℎ and orthotropic material properties, the cross-sectional stiffnesses could vary between the 
software.  
 
Figure 28 and Figure 28 outlines bending stresses in ALS design, which shows significantly larger 
variations in stresses among particularly KLH Bemessung and FEM Design, close to a variational factor 
of 3. Karamba3D results are situated almost evenly between KLH Bemessung and FEM Design results, 
however for the 110 mm panel it approximates the 2D cross-sectional calculational method of KLH 
Bemessung with an average factor of difference of ~1.3 and a higher average difference of ~2.3 
between FEM Design. Although differences in bending stress magnitudes across software are present, 
they all show similar tendency lines. Especially Karamba3D and FEM Design experience a similar 
increase in magnitude of bending stress between span length 4.5 m to 5.0 m. when inspecting the 200 
mm panel. This increase is seen in Karamba3D results for the 110 mm panel at similar span lengths,

==PAGE:41==
32 
 
yet here FEM Design outlines an almost linear plot with no similar surge in magnitude. As for the 
bending stresses in ULS, incoherences of stress magnitudes could be due to variations of cross-
sectional properties affecting the stiffness of the panel. Karamba3D does not include components 
resembling the reduced cross section methods as described in Section 3.3.7, thus depths of charred 
parts of the cross section must be manually calculated and integrated into the shell force analysis. While 
FEM Design and KLH Bemessung distinguish between minimum requirements of thicknesses of layers 
for them to contribute to the stiffness, the developed Karamba3D model does not. In cases of burned 
layers with a thickness of ≤𝑑0 = 7 𝑚𝑚, Karamba3D includes larger cross-section areas in its stress 
distribution than FEM Design and KLH Bemessung since such layer is neglected in stiffness 
calculations (Petersen, 2020). Such incongruity in methods could potentially lead to variations in section  
 
Figure 24 outlines plots of out-of-plane shear stresses. For both panel types represented, it is seen that 
KLH Bemessung encounter the highest stresses among the three software. FEM Design shows shear 
stresses which are lower than KLH Bemessung results, but higher than of what Karamba3D receives. 
This tendency between FEM Design and Karamba3D is opposite compared to bending stress plots 
shown in Figure 22, however the internal difference in shear stress magnitude is larger. Both the 200 
mm and 110 mm panel show an inclination for the Karamba3D results to be approaching the stresses 
of KLH Bemessung and FEM Design as the span length increases. This is especially outlined at spans 
of 4.5 m and 5.0, where the factor of difference is ~1,15 between FEM Design and Karamba3D results 
compared to at the short span of 3.0 m where the factor of difference is ≥2 across all three software. 
Such differences in out-of-plane shear stresses could be due several factors, including the fact that 
individual layers and shear coupling effects between glued layers is considered by default in FEM 
Design, while Karamba3D recognize the cross section as one monolithic section (StruSoft, 2023). 
Furthermore, another factor influencing the internal shear stress distribution is due to that the shell 
forces in Karamba3D are calculated before the introduction of the KLH cross-sectional data. This 
sequence of calculations can affect the resulting stresses, as the forces are a product of the cross-
sectional properties. However, since the developed script in Karamba3D does not include the option of 
importing section data before performing the FE-analysis to determine shell forces, this potential margin 
of error must be accepted.  
 
A similar tendency is seen in Figure 26 which plots rolling shear stresses. At shorter spans the stresses 
are twice or larger the magnitude in both KLH Bemessung and FEM Design, yet as the span length 
increases the shear stresses in both panels reaches a factor of difference of respectively ~1.1 between 
FEM Design and Karamba3D for the 200 mm panel and ~1.2 for the 110 mm. At the span interval 
between 3.5 m and 4.0 m the plot line of Karamba3D temporarily fluctuates from its overall trend line, 
as the rolling shear stress only slightly increases for the 110 mm panel while the stress remains 
unchanged in the 200 mm type. 
 
This change of stress-to-span ratio at around 4.0 m span is present in Figure 24 and for the shear stress 
distribution in ALS design as seen in Figure 30. While an exact explanation of this trend cannot be 
established, it can be discussed whether Karamba3D’s monolithic approach to the cross-section could 
influence the shear stress distribution. Figure 17 distinguishes from other plots, being the only chart 
where two-line plots intersect. At a span length of 4.5 m shear stresses in Karamba3D reaches 
magnitudes higher than of KLH Bemessung, while the shear stresses evens out at span length of 5.0 
m. Concurrently, shear stresses in FEM Design are significantly lower.  
 
Results of characteristic deformations in SLS design are visualised in Figure 28. Generally, deformation 
line plots across both panel types show a similar trend; FEM Design and Karamba3D have values which 
are almost equivalent to each other, while KLH Bemessung is higher. As the span length increases, the 
difference in magnitude between the software slowly increases also, however for the 110 mm panel 
==PAGE:42==
33 
 
Karamba3D and FEM Design show almost equal deflection values with respectively a deformation of 
𝑢= 34,65 𝑚𝑚 and 𝑢= 33,45 𝑚𝑚. For the 200 mm panel the difference in values at span length 5.0 m 
for Karamba3D and FEM Design is larger with 𝑢= 6,93 𝑚𝑚 and 𝑢= 8,23 𝑚𝑚. Overall, the stress plots 
for both panels follow similar inclinational trends across both panel types and software. It is critical to 
accentuate that even though the stresses follow similar trendlines, the software show varying 
differences in magnitudes of stresses. Thus, the Karamba3D model can be said to be semi-accurately 
calibrated to depict internal stresses such as bending, shear and rolling shear in both ULS and ALS. 
While such a margin of error in structural calculations generally is unacceptable and should be further 
investigated, the Karamba3D script is concluded to be satisfactory for further simulations of designs.  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:43==
34 
 
5. Baseline scenario 
As this study aims to investigate the structural possibilities within combining Cross Laminated Timber 
with either Glued Laminated Timber elements or CHS steel profiles in a composite slab structure, it is 
deemed necessary to define a baseline scenario. A baseline is defined to compare the developed 
designs and assess their flexibility and performance and exhibits what can be described as a common 
slab scenario involving an office building. From the Danish National Annex, a live load requirement 
referring to Category B of 2,5
𝑘𝑁
𝑚2 is chosen. The baseline scenario is also a vital part in the flexibility 
analysis executed in Chapter 10. To realistically depict a slab scenario, the baseline scenario 
additionally is subjected to a permanent dead load of 1 
𝑘𝑁
𝑚2 which depicts a load magnitude representing 
an arbitrary floor buildup (Dansk Standard, 2021). To enable accurate comparisons between the 
baseline scenario and the investigated design solutions, some properties defined in the baseline 
scenario are deemed invariable and act as fundamental requirements for all simulated designs. These 
requirements include the span length, the width of slab and the external dead load.

5.1 Baseline design

primary purpose of the baseline design is to serve as a reference design for evaluating the developed

6. Simulation configuration 
The simulations of the design solutions are kept in the parametric environment of Rhino Grasshopper 
utilising Karamba3D. A parametric composition ensures easily accessible configurations of the 
developed script and facilitates the potential of performing numerous calculations of various kinds at 
once. The following chapter outlines the setup and configuration of the conducted simulations. Firstly, 
an outline of the fundamental geometric concepts of the different simulated design solutions is 
presented in Section 6.2 and 6.3. These sections include subsections exhibiting overviews of the 
variable input parameters which provide the basis for the development of the different design solutions, 
both geometry and material wise. Subsequently to this, follows an introduction to the Key Performance 
Indicators (KPI’s) which will be employed in both the performance and flexibility analysis of the design 
solutions.  
 
Two main design solution categories are developed. Each category represents a vast amount of 
individual sub-designs based on different variable input parameters, which sticks to the same superior 
geometric principle of the category. A brief overview of the different design solutions is presented in

6.1 Monte Carlo sampling 
For some design categories the number of combinations of input variables has exceeded 200 million. 
Due to limited time and computer capacity, it is considered out-of-scope of this study to investigate the 
output results of all possible combinations of defined input variables. To address this issue a Monte 
Carlo randomisation technique has been applied. Monte Carlo (MC) methods are a type of 
computational algorithms which are commonly utilised to perform uncertainty and sensitivity analyses 
(Burhenne & Jacob, 2011). They rely on a randomised sampling method which can obtain numerical 
results within an investigated range of numbers.  
 
==PAGE:46==
37 
 
The limitation of this method of investigating simulating different design solutions, is that only a certain 
range of possible combinations are selected for simulation. Thus, when searching for optimal design 
solutions with regards to structural performance or flexibility, it is not guaranteed that the most optimal 
design is selected within the excessive number of possible combinations.  
 
Yet, due to its randomised character the MC method is assumed to be applicable, as it provides a 
scattershot approach on combining input variables. This method of avoiding any deterministic 
combinations is further ensured by conducting 2500-3000 simulations of different combinations within 
each design category. The concept of how the MC method is executed in Rhino Grasshopper is seen 
in Figure 33, where a cluster of pre-determined components are imported into the developed script.  
 
A summary of the applications of the most significant components of the MC method is outlined below.  
 
Cluster 
A custom component developed to run the Monte Carlo Process. The built-in algorithm 
is not further explained in this study.  
 
Domain 
Defines the domain of the combined input variables. 
 
Integer  
Defines the number of “picks” within each domain. 
 
Ladybug_fly 
A Ladybug component which enables to run through all selected combinations after 
applying the MC method. 
 
Explode tree 
Data tree containing the randomised combinations of input variables. This input data is 
linked to various parts of the script to build geometry and loading scenarios. 
 
 
Figure 33: Rhino Grasshopper worksheet of Monte Carlo (MC) component configuration 
    
 
 
==PAGE:47==
38 
 
6.2 Design solution category 1 
Category 1 of design solutions utilises five different kinds of conventional truss systems, inspired by 
designs often applied in bridge engineering. Trusses are designed to be subjected to high external 
loads, often combined with requirements for long spans with minimum deflections. Their triangulated 
shape creates an inherent strength and a stable and rigid structure which can carry large stresses (T. 
H. Chu, 2010). While traditional trusses often have top and bottom girders (beams) as elements carrying 
shear and bending, this design utilise KLH®CLT panels as plates. These are of the same width as the 
truss width. This makes conventional truss systems relevant to include in the investigation of composite 
slab performance, as they are well-known for their simple layout and their structural effectiveness. What 
distinguishes Category 1 from Category 2 is the inclusion of both an upper and lower CLT panel in the 
design. Ultimately, this makes the CLT panels functions as flanges in the cross section. The geometric 
principle of Category 1 is visualised in Figure 34. Each of the truss design patterns are outlined in 
Section 6.2.2. 
 
 
Figure 34: An arbitrary design solution within Category 1. It displays a solution utilising CHS profiles. Color tags 
reflect the distribution of compression (blue) and tension (red) in truss members.  
 
6.2.1 Variable input parameters 
Each of the design categories utilise a range of input variables to structure the individual geometry of 
the design solutions. Input variables are specific to the categories and dictates geometric properties 
such as CLT panel types, truss patterns and truss member types. A complete overview of the six input 
variables prescribing the geometry within Category 1 is presented in Table 11.

6.2.2 Truss pattern 
The truss pattern outlines the overall geometry of the slab as it portrays the layout of the internal 
members. Five different truss patterns are modeled for Design category 1, with each pattern featuring 
distinct input variables. All patterns adhere to a 2D-concept, where the position of the truss members 
does not vary in the width direction of the slab, thus always staying within the global coordinate system 
(𝑥, 𝑦, 𝑧) = (𝑥, 𝑦, 0).  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:48==

Tag 2.1 - Vierendeel Truss 
The Vierendeel truss is characterised by its vertical posts spanning between the top and bottom girder, 
creating a statically indeterminate structure. No slanted members or diagonals are applied. Vierendeel 
trusses often has an internal distribution of stresses which is more complicated than trusses with 
diagonal members due to how external forces are obtained and distributed (T. H. Chu, 2010).

6.2.5 Truss member type 
Truss members constitute the internal elements that connect the upper and lower CLT panels. While 
truss members can be constituted from a diverse range of structural members and materials, this study 
employs two distinct types of trusses in its design: Glued Laminated Timber (GL) and a Circular Hollow 
Section (CHS) steel member. Both member types are included in Karamba3D’s cross section library 
making them suitable for inclusion in this study.  
 
CHS-profiles 
CHS-profiles are common steel tubes with cross-sectional geometry as seen in Figure 45. As many 
different dimensions of such members do exist, an arbitrary range of eleven members are selected and 
included in the simulations as listed in Table 18. All members are steel quality S235. To minimise the 
==PAGE:52==
43 
 
number of iterations as explained in section 6.1, only design solutions utilising the same CHS cross 
section across the whole structure is performed, thus meaning no combinations of different steel 
sections are included in the same design solution.

6.3 Design solution Category 2 
The designs developed in Design Category 2 draws architectural and structural inspiration from truss 
systems frequently utilised in industrial buildings or stadiums, where the need for long spanning 
members is paramount. The main disparity compared to Design Category 1, is the inclusion of a bottom 
panel which is not present for the slab structure itself since a lower planar surface is not defined as a 
requirement in Category 2.  
 
In design Category 2, a total of 12 types of designs is developed. Overall, these 12 types adhere to 
three different main geometric concepts, with four sub-designs in each concept (depending on support 
conditions and truss member type). 
 
6.3.1 Geometric concept of Design solution 2.1, 2.2, 2.3 and 2.4 
Design solution 2.1 to 2.4 utilises a system of both diagonal truss members and posts. Truss members 
are internally connected at the bottom of the cross section by girders.

The alignment distance signifies the interval from the outer edge of the top panel to the centerline of

m. The alignment distance does not vary individually in each design. The alignment distance 
geometrically indicates a line parallel to the longitudinal span direction of the slab, whereas the trusses 
can connect. The principle is seen in Figure 53.

6.4 Future scenarios 
To analyse and assess the structural performance and the flexibility of the developed design solutions, 
a range of future scenarios which can influence the performance of the structure is defined. Such 
investigations enable an analysis of both how the structural response to pre-defined external influential 
factor is, and moreover how different designs react to plausible changes of environments. A range of 
such potential scenarios is established on behalf of an assessment of which external factors could be 
of relevance to include. While manifold scenarios could be of relevance to investigate four scenarios 
are included and outlined in Table 24. An argumentation for the reasoning behind selection of each 
scenario is provided.   
 
The selection process of future scenarios has been conducted by applying the Impact and probability 
matrix (Carlucci, Fioritio, & Negendahl, 2023). This tool facilitates an assessment of which future 
scenarios could be of influence to include. The process is executed by assessing the impact and 
probability of change of each considered scenario. In the case of a scenario being placed in the upper 
region with both a high impact and high probability of change, one should implement this in the flexibility 
analysis. It is vital to stress that the selection is based on an assessment, thus may not depict either 
the most impactful scenario or the one with the highest probability of change.  
 
Figure 55 illustrates the visualisation of the matrix-concept, displaying the four selected scenarios 
examined in this study. It can be observed that FS-4 and FS-3 both exhibit a higher impact and 
probability of change compared to FS-1 and FS-2. This discrepancy is further explained in subsequent 
sections and does also represent the extent to which each scenario has been investigated. Notably, 
FS-3 and FS-4 have undergone more comprehensive analysis. Moreover, FS-1 and FS-2 have 
excluded from the analyses presented in Chapter 10 and 11, yet their respective results can be found 
in Appendix 3. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:57==
48 
 
 
Figure 55: Placement of future scenarios on Impact and probability matrix (Carlucci, Fioritio, & Negendahl,

6.4.1 Change in static system 
The performance of a floor-separating slab is affected by many external factors, and its static system is 
one of them. Typology changes such as addition or removal of load-bearing walls or other structural 
members, can affect such systems. The structural response can be of high significance, as the span 
length, support conditions and their individual placement influences both stress distributions and 
deformations. One could argue that a slab structure exhibits flexibility if it is able to withstand such future 
alterations and stille provide the required structural performance. Yet, to ensure that the structure 
possesses the necessary abilities, these flexibility features must be included early in the design process. 
 
 
Figure 56: Change in static system due to removal of support 
 
The evaluation itself of the performance of a slab design based on its specific static system can be a 
challenging task. The determination of whether the system is exerting a positive or negative influence 
on the structural component can vary depending on the layout of the room or building. Removing an 
intermediate support at mid-span can drastically change the internal stress distributions and 
deformations. A simple task to numerically express this relationship is to consider a simply supported 
slab acting as a beam with an arbitrary geometry, stiffness, and an evenly distributed loading. As the 
span length increases, so does the bending moment and the deformation. Since the span length 
variable is respectively applied as 𝐿2 and 𝐿4 in the two formulas of calculating the moment and 
deformation, its influence on the structure is extensive (Jensen & Olsen, 2013). Furthermore, the 
==PAGE:58==
49 
 
removal of the intermediate support will only contribute to an increase in said stresses and 
deformations. This relationship is clearly visualised in Figure 57. Deformation at a span length of 8 m. 
increases to magnitudes almost 40 times higher, when Scenario 1 with continuous span over two spans 
is changed to Scenario 2 with one span.  
 
 
Figure 57: Relationship between bending moment, deformation, and span length. Bending moment and 
deformations calculated cf. Teknisk Ståbi (Jensen & Olsen, 2013) 
 
As mentioned, scenarios involving the structural response to an addition (FS-1B) of a support is 
neglected in both the performance and flexibility analysis. Yet, all designs options adhering to FS-1B 
have been simulated and the results of these can be accessed in Appendix 3 (Digital appendix).  
 
Table 25: Scenario tags and description for FS-1 
Scenario tag

Continuously supported over two spans

6.4.2 Integrated installations 
An essential part of the project planning is the design of ventilation systems. Buildings must have 
ventilation systems to assure that the quality of its indoor climate is sufficient. In the Danish building 
regulation BR18 a minimum inflow of 0,30 𝐿/𝑠/𝑚2 of outside air is required in residential dwellings

(Bolig- og Planstyrelsen, 2023). This transfer of air within the building is often solved by applying inter-
connected ducts. From an architectural standpoint, visible ducts are often undesirable, and are often 
tried hidden away. A floor-separating slab with either partially visible or fully hidden integrated ducts will 
in many cases arguably provide a certain aesthetic enhancement of the structure. Furthermore, the 
integration of the ducts into the slab does have the potential of reducing the total height of the slab, - 
and ceiling structure, thereby increasing floor-to-ceiling height. Figure 60 visualise two 2D-scenarios 
depicting arbitrary truss slab structures. In Scenario 1, ducts are integrated in the slab itself with no 
need for a suspended ceiling to hide the ducts. Scenario 2 includes external ducts which require a 
suspended ceiling, thereby increasing the total slab height.

investigated for FS-2 and their ability to integrate internal ducts. For this reason, FS-2 is also neglected 
in the analysis of the developed design solutions. Results can be accessed in Appendix 3 (Digital).  
 
6.4.3 Load distribution 
Another significant aspect of designing a slab structure entails consideration of external loading 
conditions. In the case of a simply supported slab with a uniform thickness t, shear and bending forces 
are often directly proportional to the magnitude of an evenly distributed line load. The magnitude and 
placement of the external loads have a direct impact on the resulting stresses and deformations 
experienced by the slab, thus consequently influencing the structural requirements of the design.  
Incorporating design strategies that allow for adaptability to varying loading conditions can enhance 
both resilience and flexibility of the structure to accommodate future changes (Negendahl, et al., 2023).  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:60==

6.4.4 Live load magnitude 
The magnitude of a load will influence the magnitude of the stresses and deformations of a structure, 
thus also affecting the requirements of the structural performance of the slab. Eurocode requires 
structures to be verified for the effects of various types of live loads, depending on the application 
category of the structure (Energistyrelsen, 2013). While a building can be designed to accommodate 
dwellings thus requiring a live load cf. DS / EN 1991-1-1 DK NA:2013 of 1,5
𝑘𝑁
𝑚2, such verifications often 
does not consider future application scenarios (e.g., change from residential to office). Such a shift in 
appliance would according to the Danish norms require an increase in live load from 1,5
𝑘𝑁
𝑚2 to 2,5
𝑘𝑁
𝑚2. 
Thus, to ensure flexibility of the structure three different scenarios depicting magnitudes of live loads 
are developed. The baseline scenario is a Category B live load of 𝑞𝑘= 1,5
𝑘𝑁
𝑚2 corresponding to a 
residential building.

6.5 Key Performance Indicators (KPI) 
To analyse the outcomes of the simulated scenarios, the output data from the simulations will be 
evaluated against KPI’s. KPI’s are established on behalf of an assessment of their influence on the 
performance of the slab. When designing slab structures there are many different structural 
performances to evaluate and consider in the process. Both ULS, SLS and ALS checks can be 
comprehensive. The included KPI’s for this study are considered the most relevant ones to include. 
Section 11.7 discusses other relevant yet neglected KPI’s.

6.5.1 Bending stress in CLT 
Bending stress due to out-of-plane loading is calculated as described in Equation 3.12. While bending 
stresses in other structural elements such as the steel or glulam girders could be of relance to be 
investigated, only the bending stresses in the CLT elements are included as a KPI. Bending stress 
distinguishes between top and bottom panels in Design category 1, where both are included.  
 
Maximum allowed bending stress is calculated as (Petersen, 2020):

= 14,75 𝑀𝑃𝑎

6.5.2 Shear stress in CLT 
Shear stress due to out-of-plane loading is calculated as described in Equation 3.16. The same principle 
as for bending stress is applied, thus only shear stresses in the CLT slab is investigated; shear stress 
in other structural elements is neglected in the assessment. Shear stress distinguishes between top 
and bottom panels in designs where both are included.
