6.5.6 Deformations 
Deformations are characterised as the total instantaneous vertical displacement of the whole slab 
structure 𝑤𝑖𝑛𝑠𝑡. In cases where the top or bottom CLT panel exhibits deformations which are larger than 
of the whole slab structure, the largest of these values are selected as the benchmark result. While the 
effects of creep and its influence on future increasement of 𝑤𝑖𝑛𝑠𝑡 to 𝑤𝑓𝑖𝑛 could have been of relevance 
to consider, it is not investigated in this study. The deflections in Karamba3D are calculated utilizing 
FEA, and do not make use of the deflection formula as seen in Equation 9.3.  
 
The maximum allowed vertical deformation 𝑤𝑖𝑛𝑠 is defined cf. recommendations in the Danish National 
Annex to Eurocode 5 (Petersen, 2020). Since these limit values are only recommendations and not 
requirements, the maximum allowed instantaneous deformation in this study is defined in Equation 6.8. 
This maximum acceptable value is smaller than what is recommended by NA but is chosen because it 
is considered a goal of this study to develop a composite slab of high structural rigidity. 
 
𝑤𝑖𝑛𝑠𝑡=
𝐿
300 = 6000 𝑚𝑚
300
= 20 𝑚𝑚 
6.8 
 
 
6.5.7 Impact sound pressure level 
The equivalent weighted normalised impact sound pressure level 𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤 is calculated as prescribed in 
Equation 3.40. 𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤 is described by means of the unit 𝑑𝐵.  
 
As mentioned in Section 3.3.8 the calculation method applied to derive 𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤 is simplified according 
to code, yet the results are assessed to be approximate and thus applicable for the analysis.  
 
6.5.8 Material consumption 
The consumption of material is a relevant benchmark parameter to investigate. While the strength of a 
structure oftentimes is directly related to the consumption of material, it is often necessary to reduce 
the consumption of material; both for economic and environmental reasons. Since different materials 
are applied, it is essential to distinguish between the three different material types applied for the 
composite slab: CLT, Glulam and steel. The materials consumption is measured by means of the unit 
𝑘𝑔.

8. Data sorting 
This chapter provides a methodical sorting of the output data from the conducted simulations. The 
sorting process is executed to eliminate and rule out design solutions, which are assessed to be unfit 
for further analysis of their structural performance and flexibility. The current dataset is comprehensive 
and includes approximately 46000 different design solutions across the different design categories. 
Thus, the objective of this process of elimination is to determine the applicable design solutions for the 
performance and flexibility analysis in Chapter 9 and Chapter 10. This process of elimination of design 
solutions is divided into three sub-processes, which is summarised in Table 31.  
 
Effectively, this chapter reduces the number of investigable design solutions from 46000 to 
approximately 450.  
 
Table 31: Summary of the elimination rounds 
Elimination round 
Summary 
1 
- 
Elimination of design solutions with KPI’s exceeding limit values defined 
in 6.5. 
2 
- 
Selection of upper and lower performing design solutions.  
- 
Subjection of nine combinations of future scenarios to every selected 
design solution 
- 
Elimination of duplicate combinations 
3 
- 
Elimination of design solutions exceeding limit values from 6.5. in any 
of the nine combinations. 
 
8.1 Elimination round 1 
The total number of developed design solutions at the current level of investigation is 46000. All of these 
are designs which depict various input variables regarding both geometry and future scenarios.  While 
numerous output data results have been computed, it is considered relevant to eliminate design 
solutions which already can be neglected in the further process due to their poor performance. The 
elimination process will be conducted by ruling out design solutions with KPI’s exceeding their defined 
limit values as described in Section 6.5. As no limit value is defined for Impact sound insulation (B7) 
and Material consumption (B8) no design solutions are eliminated due to results within these KPI’s, yet 
these parameters will still be applied in the further analysis of design options. This first elimination 
process is conducted in Microsoft Excel utilizing a simple Sort-Largest-to-Smallest function. Hereby, 
any results exhibiting exceeding KPI’s with regards to their individual limit values are eliminated.    
 
An example of a reason for elimination could be a design option which bending strength is exceeded, 
thus reaching a utilization rate of ≥100%. Such design is removed from the dataset and neglected in 
further analysis.  
 
𝐼𝐹: 𝜎𝑚,𝑥,𝑚𝑎𝑥≥𝑓𝑚,𝑑→𝐸𝑙𝑖𝑚𝑖𝑛𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑑 
 
==PAGE:66==
57 
 
 
Figure 64: Visualization of elimination principle. Designs with KPI results within the blue hatched area are 
eliminated.  
 
From the conducted sorting a total of 5585 designs solutions (approximately 12%) of the original 46000 
are eliminated. Figure 65 visualises the number of eliminated designs within each category. The chart 
shows a clear tendency:  DS1.2, 1.4, 2.2, 2.4, 2.6, 2.8, 2.10 and 2.12 generally has very few eliminated 
designs, with DS2.2 being the highest with 49. Contrary to this, the remaining designs differ from 10 to 
2206 designs which are ruled out of further analysis. This trend is to be expected, as the first mentioned 
design solutions all depict a FS-1B Scenario with a continuous slab spanning over 2 spans, while the 
latter depict FS-1A with a simply supported slab over 1 span, which greatly reduces the span length. 
From the deflection formula in Equation 9.3 and general beam theory it is known that the span length 
of a slab has a significant impact on both deformations and internal forces and stresses (Jensen & 
Olsen, 2013).  
 
 
Figure 65: Number of eliminated designs within each simulation category 
 
Furthermore, it is relevant to highlight the differences in the number of eliminated designs amongst the 
FS-1A scenarios, as it significantly fluctuates between the design types. Such variations could provide 
an early, yet approximate suggestion of which designs incorporate geometric concepts which generally 
are better performing than others with respect to their KPI’s. This is especially seen for DS2.7 which 
has almost 75% of its design solutions eliminated, while DS1.1 and 2.9 both have less than 2.5% if their 
solutions ruled out. Even though all combinations of input variables and future scenarios are

8.2 Elimination round 2 
To further reduce the number of design solutions and to narrow down on the best performing ones, a 
selection of the 25 minimum and maximum scoring design options within each KPI of all 16 design 
categories is made. The min. and max. scores are ranked and sorted, and from every KPI the 1st, 13th 
and 25th design solution is chosen. This selection principle is applied to include both low and high 
scoring designs for further analysis.  The principle of selecting 1st, 13th and 25th design solution from the 
25 ranked KPI’s is shown in Figure 68, where the max. and min. shear stresses in the top panel of 
DS1.1 is presented. A comprehensive list of all selected design solutions and their KPI results can be 
found in Appendix 3 (Digital).  
 
This process of selection is conducted through an assessment of its efficacy. While alternative methods 
could be employed, it is considered relevant to incorporate design solutions that exhibit both low and 
high scores for subsequent analysis. Thus, it is emphasised that this method does not necessarily hand-
pick the averagely highest performing design options across all KPI’s. Yet, this selection principle is 
justified by the peculiar data structure of all design solutions, which is further explained in this section.

character of the conducted Monte Carlo process all iterations are peculiar. Effectively this means that 
top performing design options potentially could be top high-ranked due to them being subjected to less 
critical combinations of input parameters and vice versa. To exemplify this, a simple comparison 
between two different input combinations and their effect on bending stresses can be made:

Equation 8.2 and 8.3 shows a significant difference in magnitude of external bending moment 𝑀𝑦 for

in Equation 3.12, it can be argued that the input combinations play a considerable role in ranking the 
designs withing their KPI. This tendency can be further supported by examining the representation and 
dispersal of benchmark criteria for an arbitrary simulation. When investigating the max. and min. 
deformations for a randomly chosen design simulation such as DS1.1, a clear pattern in the distribution 
of benchmark parameters is detected. In Figure 71 it can be observed, that for the 25 highest 
deformation values 76% of these iterations is subjected to FS-3C while 58% is subjected to FS-4A, 
which is shown to be the most critical parameters of both categories. A similar trend is seen for the 25 
designs with the lowest score, whereas 92% is subjected to FS-3A which is the lowest load magnitude 
while FS-3C is not even represented. Furthermore, 60% of the design solutions with min. deformations 
are of FS-4B, which is the least critical load distribution case with regards to bending stresses. A similar 
correlation between future scenarios and KPI results can be observed across all design solutions.

To solve this addressed combinational issue another round of simulations is conducted. The objective 
of this iteration is to subject each remaining design solution to all nine potential combinations of FS-3 
and FS-4. Thereby, it becomes possible to examine and compare the performance of the designs under 
comparable external influences of FS-3 and FS-4. This process generates a total of 5778 new 
combinations facilitating further analysis. A graphical representation of the concept is outlined in Figure

In the elimination process of each of 5778 unique combinations, a similar approach as in Section 8.1 is 
applied. This approach involves discarding design combinations that yield KPI results surpassing the 
predefined limit values. As a result of this elimination process, a total of 730 combinations are excluded 
from further analysis. Additionally, 694 combinations are removed due to them being duplicates. The 
occurrence of duplicates can be attributed to the selection process conducted in the beginning of the 
current elimination round. Certain design solutions are featured in multiple selected KPI categories, 
thereby leading to multiple inclusions of these.  
 
 
Figure 73: Number of eliminated designs within each simulation category  
 
From the initial 46000 distinctive design solutions, the total amount of remaining simulated iterations 
has been reduced to approximately 4300. It is critical to distinguish between the characteristics of these 
two sets of data. While the 46000 options are unique with regards to geometry input, the 4300 does 
indeed include multiple duplicated options with regards to geometry input. However, due to the variance 
in the subjected combinations of FS-3 and FS-4 benchmark results do fluctuate.  
 
The distribution of eliminated combinations shown in Figure 73 exhibits patterns analogue to those 
observed in Figure 65 for Elimination Round 1. Once again, DS2.5 and 2.7 demonstrate the highest

must be discarded before further analysis. This process is executed in Elimination Round 3.  
 
8.3 Elimination round 3 
Due to the nature of the sorting and elimination process of Elimination Round 2, the remaining data 
after this round are combinations of design solutions and future scenarios. Effectively, the effect of this 
is that remaining design solutions are included from one to nine times, depending on how many of their 
individual combinations were eliminated in Elimination Round 2. As aforementioned, design solutions 
with KPI’s surpassing defined maximum limits within any of the nine combinations are discarded. This 
separation is conducted in Elimination Round 3, once again executed in Microsoft Excel. The amount 
of residual design solutions within the different categories after elimination are shown in Figure 74. As 
seen, the designs from DS1.1 to DS1.4 generally have more designs left, while the count of DS2.1 to 
DS2.12 does fluctuate more. Only DS2.7 has every of its design solutions ruled out.  
 
 
Figure 74: Remaining design solutions after Elimination Round 3.  
 
Conclusively, the three rounds of eliminations have reduced the number of design solutions from circa 
46000 to a total remaining count of 448. These remaining designs are analyzed for their performance 
and flexibility in the forthcoming sections.

9. Structural analysis  
An analysis of the design solutions is a compulsory part of investigating both their flexibility and 
structural performance. The analysis is divided into two separate chapters, to provide a simple 
composition. This chapter analyse the design solutions on behalf of their structural performance 
employing the KPI’s as benchmarks. This includes investigation of the combinations derived from 
Elimination Round 2 and the remaining designs solutions procured in Elimination Round 3. Chapter 10 
analyse the design solutions with regards to flexibility.  
 
In following sections an outline of the remaining design solutions and their performance within the 
individual KPI’s is presented. The aim of the analysis is to investigate which design options perform well 
within the different KPI’s. The baseline design results as outlined in Table 9, are introduced for the first 
time in the analysis. These results are employed to function as guiding principles, when analysing the 
results of the developed design solutions. Due to the amount of KPI’s and the manifold individual 
combinations of relationships between such, only selected KPI’s and relationships are analysed. The 
selection of these is executed on behalf of an assessment of their relevance to the overall performance 
of the developed slab design solutions.  
 
9.1 Deformation 
When designing slab structures, dealing with deformations is a very significant part of the design 
process of the Serviceability Limit State (SLS). While no concrete requirements are defined by 
Eurocodes, the limit values for deformations are specific to the project and can be stricter than code 
recommendations. Thus, deformations are assessed to be a significant KPI to highlight.  
 
Figure 75 shows the distribution of deformations amongst all designs subjected to FS-1A. It is seen that 
all designs have few combinations which are close to 𝑤𝑚𝑎𝑥,𝑖𝑛𝑠𝑡= 20 𝑚𝑚, but generally most of the 
designs yield deformations below 5 mm. Only DS2.1 has a maximum deformation of below 15 mm. 
Generally, the medians do not fluctuate much between designs, with almost all values for designs 
emerging below 5 mm. Only DS2.5 has a median which is breaking this trend, as it approaches a 
median of approximately 8mm.  DS 2.1, DS2.3, DS2.9, DS2.11 and DS1.1 all have medians below 5 
mm, while DS1.3 is just above. The inter-quartile ranges (IQR) of DS2.1, DS2.3, DS2.11 and DS1.1 are 
significantly smaller than of the remaining design solutions. This could be an indication of these designs 
to be specifically good at resisting deformations at varying load magnitudes (FS-3) and distributions 
(FS-4). DS2.7 is not included in the box plot since all its designs were eliminated in Elimination Round 
3.  
 
 
==PAGE:73==
64 
 
 
Figure 75: Box plot of deformations for design solutions of FS-1A. Results are combinations sourced from 
Elimination Round 2.  
 
It is clearly seen how all design solutions have medians which are lower than the median of the baseline 
design. This distribution of deformations illustrates that at least 50% of the developed design solutions 
exhibit a structural rigidity for showed combinations, which is better in deflection resistance than 50% 
of the baseline scenarios. However, as Figure 75 illustrates combinations of scenarios sourced from 
Elimination Round 2, the chart essentially compares results of deformations across different 
combinations of scenarios. Thus, this frame of reference is not accurate enough for a 1:1 comparison 
between the developed design solutions and the baseline and can only be said to illuminate a vague 
trend of distribution. To accurately compare, the relationship between deformations and mass is 
introduced in the following section.  
 
9.1.1 Deformation and mass correlation 
The investigation of the relationship between deformations and the mass of the materials used for the 
composite slab designs, is assumed to be relevant to conduct. The mass of the design solutions is an 
important KPI, since it is linked to both economic and environmental aspects of the design. In this 
context, it is also relevant to examine the differentials in deformations between the simulated composite 
designs and the baseline scenario. From observing Figure 75, it is clearly seen that each of the 
simulated designs exhibits combinations that result in deformations lower than what is observed for the 
baseline. This observation is particularly relevant, due to this indicating a high probability of having 
simulated designs solutions outperforming the baseline design with regards to deformations. To 
accurately compare and draw such conclusions, a comparative analysis of the differences in 
deformations between the baseline design and a range of the simulated designs solutions is conducted, 
while identical combinations of FS-3 and FS-4 are considered.  
 
For this purpose, the design solutions are divided into two categories: those employing glulam in the 
truss system, and those utilising CHS profiles. Two separate plots are developed for each of the 
categories. The total mass of the design solutions is represented on y-axis, while the deformation 𝑤𝑖𝑛𝑠𝑡 
is plotted on the x-axis. When calculating the mass GL and CLT are treated as identical materials, due 
to their similar properties. Additionally, the mass of the CHS profiles is also added to the total mass 
represented on the y-axes. While CLT and CHS are two very different materials, for this analysis the 
mass is treated as a total.  
 
==PAGE:74==
65 
 
 
Figure 76: Mass and deformation chart DS2.1, DS2.5, DS2.9, DS1.1 and the baseline design 
 
Figure 76 shows a scattering of DS2.1, 2.5, 2.9, 1.1 and Baseline results. The baseline design is seen 
as the orange dot with a mass of approximately 612 kg and a deformation close to 20 mm when 
subjected to FS-3A and FS-4. An interesting observation to derive from this figure is the range of design 
simulations with both a mass and deformation lower than of the baseline design. All these designs are 
plotted in the hatched area of the chart. These designs can be said to be more optimal than the baseline 
when considering mass and deformation, however not necessarily with respect to each other. It is seen 
that the different design solution categories lie within different regions of the scatter plot. Generally, 
DS1.1 designs yield low deformations combined with a high mass compared to other design solutions. 
DS2.1 shows an almost linear relationship between its mass and deformation, with its values emerging 
between both higher and lower scoring designs. DS2.5 is represented only by nine remaining design 
solutions, where none of these are optimal with regards to deformation or mass. Lastly, DS2.9 shows 
a very clear tendency of offering both low deformations combined with low mass. Even though its 
deformation values fluctuate a lot, with nine design solutions emerging at above 𝑤𝑖𝑛𝑠𝑡= 15 𝑚𝑚, these 
designs utilise low CLT and GL mass.  
 
To investigate and plot the most efficient designs a Pareto frontier is drawn in Figure 76. A Pareto 
frontier can visualize a range of non-dominated design options, whereas each of the options are 
uniformly satisfactory. This essentially means, that if one objective is to minimize the mass of the slab, 
it will come at the expense of the deformation or vice versa. Thus, the Pareto frontier can identify a set 
of slab design solutions which are optimal either with regards to mass or deformation. (Ngatchou, Zarei, 
& El-Sharkarwi, 2005). The Pareto frontier clearly indicates that DS2.9 offers the most optimal designs 
if both a low mass and deformation is the objective of the design process. In fact, none of the other 
design solutions are present at the range of the frontier with reduced mass. However, if the objective 
were to reduce deformations to the possible minimum and simultaneously accepting an exceeding of 
the mass of the baseline result, DS1.1 would be the best design option. These DS1.1 data points are 
represented at very small deformation values, but with masses which are relatively high and larger than 
the baseline design.

From Figure 77 it can be observed that DS2.11 represents most of the optimal designs compared to 
the baseline. A total of four DS2.11 designs on the drawn Pareto frontier. Almost all designs of DS2.3 
and DS2.11 outperform the baseline with respect to both mass and deformation. DS1.3 scores relatively 
low deformation yields on many of its design, however these values come at the expense of high 
masses, almost all higher values than of the baseline design. Only three DS1.3 designs are within the 
hatched area where both deformation and mass are lower than the baseline. When observing the chart, 
it is important to emphasise that the mass represents a sum of both CLT and CHS-profiles, thus 
reflecting different materials and with different strength properties.  
 
From the analysis of both Figure 76 and Figure 77 one of the main observations is that designs of type 
DS2.9 and 2.11, which employs an identical structural concept, performs the best when comparing 
deformations and masses. This is true for both the design utilising CHS profiles and the one using GL. 
This trend across truss member materials indicates that the geometric concept of these designs 
structurally has a high rigidness. Thereby, these design solutions demonstrate an efficient internal force 
distribution, thus allowing for an optimal resistance of large deformations combined with relatively low 
utilisation of materials.  
 
For further analysis and to allow for a final estimation of the lead performing design solutions across 
investigated KPI’s, all designs placed on the two Pareto frontiers in Figure 76 and Figure 77 are 
recorded. Only designs outperforming the baseline design are tabbed. A preliminary overview of such 
designs is outlined in Table 34.

9.2 Bending, shear and rolling shear stress 
Investigation of internal stresses in Ultimate Limit State (ULS) is one of the main processes of designing 
CLT panels. Contrary to examination of deformations in SLS, requirements for stress limits can be 
Eurocode requirements when documenting the performance of slab structures. However, since CLT 
still is to be included in the EC’s, requirements for stress limits are often provided by the manufacturer. 
This is also valid for CLT delivered by KLH Massivholz which product properties applies to ETA-06/0138 
(European Technical Assessment, 2021).  
 
9.2.1 Bending stress 
In Figure 78 the dispersion of bending stresses across designs solutions within FS-1A is visualised. 
When observing the box plot it is seen that the medians of all design solutions is lower than of the 
baseline design. DS1.1, 2.11 and 2.9 have the lowest medians, while DS2.11 also shows the smallest 
IQR across all design solutions. DS2.5 yields a slightly larger IQR whilst also having a max. bending 
stress value significantly larger than of the other design solutions. Another interesting observation to 
derive is that the baseline design also has a max. stress which is notably smaller than of many of the 
design solutions. It can be insinuated that the reason for this is, that the outlier bending stress values 
occurs in design solution iterations which utilises CLT panels thinner than the baseline design. This 
difference in thickness and lamella build-up directly influences the moment of inertia of the cross 
section, thereby leading to a reduced rigidness. A relationship which is outlined in Equation 3.9.  
 
 
Figure 78: Box plot of bending stress for FS-1A.

and FS-4A, which in most cases is assessed to produce the most critical bending stresses as explained 
in Equation 8.2 and 8.3.

bending stresses and the mass of the baseline design. DS1.1 generally yield design with low bending

Shear and rolling shear stress 
In CLT design the investigation of shear stress and rolling shear stress are two processes of similar 
character. Grashof’s formula as seen in Equation 3.14 is utilised for determining the critical shear force, 
while the static moment of the cross section depends on the lamella direction, thickness, and sequence 
in build-up. This equivalence is outlined in Equation 3.16 and 3.19, whereas the notable difference is in 
the layer of investigation. While shear stresses 𝜏𝑣,𝑥𝑧,𝑑 are critical in the longitudinal layer the rolling 
shear stress 𝜏𝑣,𝑅,,𝑧𝑥,𝑑 is to be checked in the transverse lamellas closest to the neutral axis of the cross-
section. This principle is visualised for a 5-layer CLT panel in Figure 81. The two semi-identical formulas 
for calculating the two different types of stresses yield shear and rolling shear stresses which are 
uniform in magnitude. Yet, as the difference in strength properties of 𝑓𝑣,𝑅,𝑘= 1,2 𝑀𝑃𝑎 and 𝑓𝑣,𝑘= 2,7 𝑀𝑃𝑎 
are significant the critical check to perform is often of rolling shear. Due to the discrepancy in strength

9.2.2 Sound pressure level 
One of the least analysed KPI’s yet is the weighted normalised impact sound pressure level. As for 
stresses and deformations, a slab structure will almost always be met by requirements for its impact 
sound insulation. Especially for slab structures dividing different dwellings requirements can be strict. 
In the Danish Building Regulation, BR18, this requirement is 𝐿′𝑛,𝑤≤53 𝑑𝐵. Since the calculation of 
𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤 in this study has been simplified as explained in Section 3.3.8, the calculated impact sound 
values must be treated as approximate values. Yet, these values are assessed to guiding in evaluating 
the acoustic performance of the developed design solutions. 
 
Due to the nature of Equation 3.40, the impact sound level is semi-inversely proportional to the mass 
of the structure. Effectively this means, that as the mass of the slab increases 𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤 decreases, thus 
improving the impact sound insulation. This relationship can be expressed by plotting values of 
𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤 and the mass for a random selected design solution:  
 
 
Figure 87: Relationship between 𝑳𝒏,𝒆𝒒,𝟎,𝒘 and mass of the design for DS2.1. 
 
While a low mass of the structure in the analysis of design solutions has been desired so far, when 
including 𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤 in the design requirements, it adds another KPI which can be optimized for. In Figure 
88 it is seen, that as deformation increases so does 𝐿𝑛,𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤. However, what is not visualised in this 
chart is the mass of the optimal solution. Yet, from Figure 87 it is known that this optimal solution with 
regards to impact sound insulation and deformation will not be optimal in its use of mass.

9.3 Sub-conclusion 
This section presents a range of sub-conclusions based on the previous analyses. The conclusion is 
outlined in Chapter 15. 
 
- 
Most design solutions outperform the baseline design on KPI’s such as deformation and 
the various stress types.  
 
- 
DS2.9 and DS2.11 are the best performing design solution categories, when the objective 
of the design process is to minimise either deformation and mass or various stress types 
and mass.   
 
- 
DS1.1 generally yields the lowest deformations and stresses, yet this comes at the expense 
of an averagely high mass compared to other design solutions.   
 
- 
A high mass deduces a low impact sound pressure which is optimal in absorbing impact 
sounds. Due to DS1.1 and DS1.3’s relatively high mass because of their double-layered 
CLT panel design, such designs generally perform well in acoustic context.  
 
- 
DS2.1, DS2.3 and DS2.5 perform averagely when analysing correlations of either 
deformations and mass or stress and mass. No individual designs within these design 
categories are optimal with regards to such KPI’s.  
 
- 
A semi-linear relationship between bending stresses and deformations are seen for DS2.3, 
DS2.11 and DS1.3. While a similar relationship between either shear stress and 
deformations or rolling shear stress and deformations have not been investigated, a similar 
trend is expected to occur for these. Furthermore, this relationship is expected to emerge 
if other designs solutions were to be investigated.  
 
- 
Two individual designs of DS2.9 and DS2.11 appear across all Pareto fronts. This is DS2.9 
- 14 and DS2.11 - 12. Both design solutions are summarised and visualised below. Both 
designs have the same geometry input variables except for their truss member type.

9.3.1 Optimal design solutions 
The two optimal designs solutions which appear across all Pareto frontiers are visualised in this 
section.  
 
DS2.9 - 14 
DS2.9 - 14 incorporates a KLH ® 60mm 3s TL deck, which is the thinnest CLT slab amongst included 
panels. The specific CLT slab is complemented by a 140x80 mm GL truss, comprising a total height of 
0,9m. The truss configuration of DS2.9 utilises the maximum possible truss count, featuring a total of 
10 connections at the bottom girder (comprising 20 truss members in total). Consequently, this design 
yields a rigid truss system, with a weight of 163,5 kg. However, due to the slim CLT panel, the overall 
mass is comparatively low, totalling 347 kg.

10. Flexibility analysis 
This section aims to analyse and assess the flexibility of the developed design solutions. As no 
acknowledged methods of directly measuring the flexibility of a load-bearing structure exist, the applied 
method is developed for this specific cause. The approach draws inspiration from a method developed 
by K. Negendahl, F. Carlucci and F. Fiorito, which proposes a technique to measure the energy flexibility 
of a shading system (Carlucci, Fioritio, & Negendahl, 2023). The theoretical framework of the developed 
method is outlined in Section 3.1.5. 
 
Due to the number of remaining designs, only a selected range of the design solutions are analysed 
with regards to their flexibility. Firstly, an example on how to apply the method of deriving 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖 as 
outlined in Section 3.1.5 is presented in Section 10.1.1.

chosen as an example design. Six of the developed eight KPI’s are introduced in this analysis: bending, 
shear, rolling shear, tension, compression, and deformation of the slab designs. The future scenarios 
are combined in a matrix, which outlines the KPI’s results for each of the nine combinations. A summary 
of these is outlined in Table 39.

in Figure 4. All cases of KPI’s have their 1st quartile and median below zero. This dispersion implies 
that more than 50% of the results are lower than of the baseline, which is valid for all KPI’s. In general, 
a design can be said to be more flexible, when the impact on the KPI of the subjected scenario is low. 
As the impact increases, the flexibility of the design will decrease. Overall, the box plots across all KPI’s 
yields similar IQR, median placement, and whisker placement. This similarity supports the assumption 
of how the individual KPI result magnitudes react in the same manner, when subjected to different 
scenarios. Only compression has a 3rd quartile value which is significantly higher than of the other five 
KPI’s.

Established box plots for results of Equation 3.4 and 3.3 show corresponding trends to results derived 
by Equation 3.2b. This resemblance is expected, as the two methods are similar in procedure. Once 
again, all six KPI’s still classify as Class 3, with both their median and 1st quartile below zero. The IRQ 
is also similar in size yet cannot be directly compared across all KPI’s as these possess different units.  
The major difference between the two methods is how the results are presented. Whilst Example 1 
utilise normalised values, Example 2 illuminate the dispersion showcasing the values with units of the 
different investigated KPI’s. This way of presenting how the design solution performs within the KPI’s 
when subjected to different scenarios, makes Equation 3.4c and 3.3d a robust method for a quick 
analysis. Moreover, it enables the designer to quickly develop an overview of the design performance 
of the KPI’s as it includes units. 
 
10.1.3 Comparison of best performing designs 
Three design solutions are selected for a comparison of their flexibility. The designs are selected due 
their performance illuminated in the structural analysis in Chapter 9. Moreover, the baseline design is 
introduced for comparison also (tension and compression is excluded). Each of them represents a 
design category: DS2.9 - 14, DS2.11 - 12, and DS1.1 - 5.  The method outlined in 10.1.1 utilising 
Equation 3.2b is applied.

The developed boxplots showing the dispersion of KPI results for three selected design solutions exhibit 
very similar tendencies. Especially DS2.9 - 14 and DS2.11 - 12 are almost identical across all six KPI’s 
with small variances in their medians, the IQR and the placement of their whiskers. For shear and rolling 
shear stresses DS2.9 - 14 demonstrates a whisker which is located at a slightly higher value, however 
this difference is assessed to be of minor significance. Generally, DS1.1 - has almost identical medians 
with regards to shear, rolling shear, bending and deformation, yet both 1st and 3rd quartile is slightly 
dislocated compared to the other two designs. All designs adhere to a Class 3 with regards to the 
classification system Figure 4, which is also observed to be the case for the majority of the developed 
design solutions when analysing the results across all designs. Only DS1.1 - 5 is located with a median 
which is placed exactly at zero for the tensile stresses, thus adhering between a classification of Class 
3 and Class 4. This placement could potentially be due to a minor mistake in the calculation of the 
tensile stress for this exact combination, however this is not further investigated.  
 
Moreover, the baseline designs have been incorporated as a reference point or benchmark point for 
comparison. Across all four plots, it is observed that the baseline design demonstrates a smaller IQR 
compared to the developed designs. This distinction is assessed to be attributed to the utilisation of a 
200mm 5s CLT pale in the baseline design, while the developed design solutions employ considerably 
thinner CLT panels. When observing the bending and rolling shear stress, the medians of the baseline 
designs is higher than of the other designs. Additionally, the median of the baseline design is almost 
identical to the other designs shear and deformation charts.  
 
10.2 Measuring flexibility of multiple design solutions 
The conducted comparison is an effective tool for measuring the flexibility of several designs at once, 
however becomes more complicated and time-consuming as the number of designs increase.  This 
section utilises the method presented in Equation 3.5, to measure and compare the flexibility of multiple 
design solutions at once.  
 
To measure the flexibility and compare across design solutions, plots visualising the relationship 
between the median and the IQR for each of the six KPI’s is developed. Due to the difference in the 
calculation of the tensile and compressive stress of the GL and CHS, the plots for design solutions 
utilising each of these two truss types must be executed separately. The plots are scattered for FS-1A 
scenarios, while FS-2B is neglected in this analysis. The most flexible design (from conducting this 
method) is marked in each KPI chart.  
==PAGE:90==
81 
 
 
Generally, a clear and almost linear tendency between the median and the IQR is present. This 
coherence is visible across all six KPI’s. Such relationship suggests that as the spread of the data 
increases or decreases, the central tendency also changes proportionally. So as the results become 
more spread out, the median tends to increase or decrease in a consistent manner. This behaviour 
illustrates a systematic relationship between the spread and the central tendency when the data is 
systematically distributed around the median. Furthermore, it is observed that the data points 
representing the various design solutions exhibit a wide dispersion around the linear trend line. 
However, certain design solutions exhibit notably low values in terms of both their median and IQR. In 
accordance with the definition of high flexibility, these designs are deemed to possess a flexible nature. 
 
 
Figure 109: Bending stress flexibility chart for FS-1A 
 
In Figure 109 it is seen how DS1.1 dominates the region of both a low median and a low IQR. A total 
of eight DS1.1 designs solutions outperform other design categories, with a few DS2.9 results placed 
close by. Moreover, DS1.3 and DS2.11 also demonstrates quite a few design solutions which are in the 
lower region of the median and IQR. DS2.5 and DS2.3 is generally appearing in the upper, less flexible 
region of the chart.  
 
Figure 109 also visualise the location of the baseline design on the chart, placed almost in the between 
the upper and lower extremes across designs. Considering the method employed to measure flexibility, 
a significant number of design solutions demonstrate higher flexibility than the baseline design, 
especially in relation to bending stresses. Similar trends are observed in Figure 110 and Figure 111 for 
shear and rolling shear stresses, where the baseline design are placed even closer to the upper 
extreme, reflecting the least flexible design solution. The baseline design exhibits the least flexibility 
among all design solutions, specifically in the KPI category depicting deformation, where it is ranked 
among the 15 designs with the lowest flexibility.

11. Discussion 
This chapter discusses and reflects on the applied theoretical framework and the results of both the 
flexibility and structural performance analysis.  
11.1 Development of new load-bearing structures 
When embarking on the design process of a new load-bearing structure, numerous factors need to be 
considered. Firstly, it is crucial to determine the necessity of the design and whether there is a demand 
for such structure. New load-bearing structures are often created to fulfill specific need(s). These could 
be both environmental, economic, structural, or architectural. Once the needs(s) have been identified, 
the designer should establish a reference point to investigate which specific properties the design must 
deliver to provide a solution for the defined need.  
In this study, the objective is to develop a range of design solutions to enact as slab structures by 
combining CLT with various truss systems. These design solutions are intended to explore and tackle 
multiple aspects related to the performance of CLT slab structures. One specific objective which is 
investigated, is how the total mass of a conventional CLT slab structure can be reduced by introducing 
trusses, while still meeting the structural and architectural performance requirements stipulated by 
standards and producers. The potential reduction in utilised mass is assessed to not only lower 
economic costs, as CLT is often sold per m3, but also to decrease the consumption of timber resources 
applied in a such structure (CBI - Ministry of Forein Affairs, 2017).   
 
Moreover, the objective is to develop and investigate a method for measuring flexibility of a load-bearing 
structures. A state-of-the-art method on measuring the energy consumption of a building component, 
is utilised as inspiration for the proposal on an approach to measure flexibility of a load-bearing 
structure.  
 
11.2 Performance and flexibility 
Based on the findings of the analysis conducted in Chapter 9, a trend emerges among the types of 
design solutions demonstrating an optimal performance. Especially, within the investigated 
relationships between the sum of mass and either stresses or deformations, design solutions within 
DS2.9 and DS2.11 consistently occupy the Pareto optimal front. Only in one case does a third type of 
design solution appear, DS1.1, which is in the examination of the correlation between rolling shear 
stress and mass. These results strongly indicate that DS2.9 and DS2.11 embody a geometric concept 
which excels in the distribution of forces and in the resistance of deformations. What characterise DS2.9 
and DS2.11, which both share the same common geometric concept, is the inclusion of two truss ends 
in every connection between the panel and glulam truss. Only at the end of the panel is these joints 
connected by only one truss end.  This design feature enables an almost direct transfer of tension and 
compression between the truss members without the need for transferring such forces as bending and 
shear within the CLT panel.  An outcome of this design concept is assessed to be, that the bending and 
shear stresses that would otherwise be absorbed by the CLT plate are partially relieved, as the truss 
system obtain and carry a significant part of these forces as axial forces. Due to the truss layout and 
the member geometry, they are better suited to handle these types of loads compared to the CLT, which 
is more prone to bending and shear (T. H. Chu, 2010).  
 
In the highlighting of the designs considered optimal in the analysis, it is important to stress that these 
only are optimal with regards to their relationship between mass and the investigated KPI’s. If deriving 
the lowest possible KPI were the objective, DS1.1 would outperform DS2.9 and 2.11 in both bending, 
shear, rolling shear, and deformation.  This is indeed an interesting observation, as the assumption 
made in the flexibility analysis in Chapter 10 outlines that when the median and IQR decrease, the 
==PAGE:95==
86 
 
flexibility of the structure increases and vice versa. Essentially, this could indicate a relationship 
between the mass and the flexibility: the higher mass, the more flexible the structure is. In other words, 
the designer could be contrived to add extra mass for the structure to be able to withstand potential 
future scenarios.  
 
However, the suggestion of the correlation between mass and the flexibility is not necessarily valid for 
all designs. In Chapter 9 on the structural analysis it was shown how DS2.9 - 14 and DS2.11 - 12 both 
occurs on every Pareto optimal frontier in the investigations on the different KPI’s relationship to the 
mass. To show that both the median and IQR can be low for a KPI (meaning a high flexibility) while the 
mass is low compared to other designs, the position of DS2.9 - 14 and DS2.11 - 12 can be pointed out 
as seen in Figure 117. This figure only illustrates the position of DS2.9 - 14 and DS2.11 - 12 on the 
deformation chart, yet it is assessed that a similar placement of for bending, shear, and rolling shear 
stress would occur, as both designs perform well within these KPI’s also.  
 
 
Figure 117: Position of DS2.11 - 12 and DS2.9 - 14 on the flexibility plot 
Figure 118 
Effectively, this chart illustrates that designs can be optimal both with regards to performance and 
flexibility. And since the flexibility is measured as a function of the performance, the flexibility is directly 
dependent on the investigated performances. This observation derives a recommendation to also 
include KPI’s such as mass, CO2-footprint, or costs for assessing flexibility, as these necessarily aren’t 
directly affected by the performance in the same way as stresses, forces, deformations etc. are.  
 
Moreover, this tendency underlines that many factors must be taken into consideration by the designer, 
when calculating the flexibility of a load-bearing structure. As described in Chapter 3, one of the main 
objectives of flexibility is to prolong the lifespan of the investigated structure, by making it adaptable to 
future changes. This purpose of avoiding potential replacements or demolition of the structure saves 
resources and CO2. Yet, this extra addition of mass does increase the initial CO2-emission of the 
structure due to factors such as increased embodied carbon and potential extra transport logistics. One 
could then argue that the addition of extra mass to provide necessary flexibility makes no sense, if the 
negative effect of this initial action is larger than avoiding it.   
This discussion is essential, as it extends beyond the investigated designs solutions of this study. The 
concept could reflect a scenario of incorporating additional mass in other structural members as well. 
DS2.11 - 12
DS2.9 - 14
0
2
4
6
8
10

An example is a reinforced concrete beam incorporating extra steel bars or a concrete column utilising 
higher concrete quality (e.g., from C30 to C45), to overcome the impact of potential future changes. 
Both reflect design choices, which serves the purpose of enhancing their initial capacity to also 
withstand future transformations etc. It is relevant to stress, that such modifications have both 
environmental and economic costs, and that the assessment of these costs falls within the responsibility 
of the designer to consider. 
 
A simple example of such a scenario is visualised in Figure 119. It shows the CO2/year footprint of a 
“non-flexible” structure, which due to future requirements such as typology changes or extreme weather 
events does need replacement at a given time. Conversely, a “flexible” structure incorporates the ability 
to accommodate such future changes from its initial design features. However, such ability does come 
at the expense of a higher CO2-footprint at the production of this structure. Nonetheless, the flexibility 
of the design allows it to effectively overcome future scenarios, and thereby induce a lower CO2/year 
footprint over its entire lifespan compared to the non-flexible design.  
 
 
Figure 119: ”Flexible” vs. ”Non-flexible” solution. “Flexible” structure solutions has lower CO2/year/structure 
value at end of lifespan compared to “Non-flexible” alternative.  
 
Contrary to Figure 119, an opposite scenario could also be thought of. In such case the addition of extra 
initial mass requires more CO2 or extra economic costs at the production stage, and this extra 
supplement of resources (environmentally or economic) would never pay off as the alternative of the 
replacement is better. Additionally, a scenario could be introduced where a future replacement type of 
a load-bearing structures becomes more sustainable than existing ones (or even 100% CO2-neutral) 
due to rapidly developing technologies and recycling strategies. Such projection on the development of 
technologies, was amongst one of the arguments by Danish Klima-, Energi- og Forsyningsministeriets 
for the ability of Denmark as a nation to reach the by- law-required reduction of GHG-emissions by 70% 
before 2030 (Retsinformation, 2023). This scenario is depicted in Figure 120. It can be observed how 
the CO2-neutral replacement does not affect the CO2/year value, thus outperforming both the “Flexible” 
solution and the other “Non-flexible” replacement alternative.

11.3 Flexibility measurement methodology  
The developed methodology for measuring the flexibility in this study can be discussed with regards to 
its procedure and possible accuracy. Overall, the method of utilising a classification system presented 
in Figure 4 are evaluated to be an effective tool in measuring flexibility. While flexibility in construction 
and architecture for years has been discussed as a design thought-of-mind, the research on a method 
of measuring the flexibility of a load-bearing structure is very limited. The proposed method of 
calculating a normalised KPI performance value,  𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖  inspired by the method of deriving a 
normalised energy consumption value 𝑁𝐸𝐶𝑉𝑘𝑖 is thought to be applicable in the measurement and 
assessment of a load-bearing structure: 
 
𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖= 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝑆𝑖−𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝐶𝑆
𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝐶𝑆
 
11.1 
 
However, there are several limitations that need to be addressed regarding the proposed approaches 
for measuring flexibility as outlined Section 3.1.5. Firstly, it should be noted that the development 
process of boxplots for 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖 for manifold load-bearing structures at once is assessed to be both time-
consuming and comprehensive. Additionally, the subsequent analysis of multiple boxplots can be 
extensive and resource intensive.  
 
Another limitation worth highlighting is that when  𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖  is measured for a range of load-bearing 
structures subjected to the same future scenarios, the KPI results (such as bending, shear, rolling shear, 
and deformations) will often either decrease or increase in the same direction, if the designs are similar 
in geometry. This coherence arises due to the inherent nature of determining stress and deformation 
calculations as presented in Section 3.3. A coherence which inflicts KPI results and thus also 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖. 
As a result, boxplots across designs tend to become similar in appearance, thus being challenging to 
interpret and compare effectively. Furthermore, their classification with regards to Figure 4 will be the 
same in most cases, with only slight variations in the placement of the upper and lower whiskers being 
the indication of differences in flexibility between the investigated designs. One could then argue that it 
CO2 / year / structe
Time
"Flexible" vs. "Non-flexible" structure
Flexible-structure
Non-flexible structure
Non-flexible - 100% CO2-neutral
Replacement
==PAGE:98==
89 
 
is reasonable for semi-identical designs to yield similar flexibility results, yet a more numerical approach 
on interpreting the results could potentially enhance the efficiency of the comparison process.   
 
The alternative approach outlined in Equation 3.5 plots medians and the IQR’s for individual KPI’s and 
is proposed as an alternative method due its perceived efficiency. Notably, it differs from the 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖-
Classification system depicted in Figure 4 in terms of considering flexibility. In the Median/IQR-plotting 
method, the evaluation of the flexibility of analysed design solutions relies solely on the magnitude of 
both the median and the IQR. Consequently, the position of this data point on the developed chart of 
the investigated KPI can be interpreted and compared in relation to other design. However, this 
approach is assessed to have its limitations also. Since this method only includes the IQR between 1st 
quartile and 3rd quartile, it effectively rules out results placed in quartile group 1 and quartile group 4. 
This way of interpreting the data does neglect the extreme results, both lower and upper. Moreover, it 
also allows to only consider the 50% (quartile group 2 and 3) results around the median, which though 
in many cases are assessed to the one of highest relevance to include due to their probability of 
happening. This neglection of extreme results can be discussed to provide a one-sided measurement 
of flexibility.  
 
Furthermore, the way that the Median/IQR-plotting method measures flexibility is different to the 
normalisation process of the 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖 equations. This difference in interpreting flexibility means, that what 
is considered “flexible” in the Median/IQR-plotting method is not necessarily considered “flexible” with 
regards to the 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖−Classification system. To demonstrate this, four arbitrary datapoints are chosen 
along the trendline of the bending stress chart. The boxplot for each of them is drawn utilising Equation 
3.2.

The boxplots show that three of the designs, DS1.1 - 21, DS1.3 - 26 and DS2.3 - 2 classifies as Class 
3, while DS2.1 - 16 refers to Class 4. This is a vital observation as DS2.3 - 2 with regards to the 
Median/IQR-plotting method exhibits a less flexible character than of DS2.1 - 16, however is more 
flexible when utilising the 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖-Classification system. This same dispersion is strongly assumed to be 
occurring for other KPI’s and between other design solutions as well.  
 
From shown examples, it is emphasized that the 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖-method and the Median/IQR-plotting 
illuminates the flexibility from two different perspectives. Thus, they cannot both be applied in the same 
measurement of flexibility and results cannot be directly compared due to this difference in perspective. 
Yet, both are assessed to be bilaterally applicable in the measurement of flexibility of a load-bearing 
structure. A proposal for an effective utilisation of the method in case of multiple design solutions, is to 
first apply the Median/IQR-plotting method to provide an overview in the dispersion of the defined KPI’s. 
From this chart, some of the lower-scattered data-points could be cherry picked for a further analysis 
using the 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖-Classification method.  
 
11.4 Selection of future scenarios 
The selection process of future scenarios is assessed to be a crucial process of measuring the flexibility, 
as they are decisive for how the distribution of results are. Future scenarios will often be the significant 
unknown parameter of flexibility measurement process, as an assessment of their probability always 
will be somehow subjective to the designer. In many cases a strong tool to foresee upcoming events is 
by analysing data of past events. For typology changes such as load magnitudes or distributions, one 
==PAGE:100==
91 
 
could consider data on demolitions and refurbishments. Another future scenario which is assessed to 
potentially create large effects on load-bearing structures are extreme weather events, yet these are 
neglected in this study. For such assessments, machine-learning or Artificial Intelligence (AI) algorithms 
could be utilised to predict how such future changes more accurately could unfold (Huntingford, 2019).  
 
Furthermore, the number of included scenarios in the measurement process is assessed to affect the 
flexibility results. As the number of future scenarios increases, so does the likelihood of the flexibility 
results to differ amongst each other, in the case of multiple load-bearing structures being compared at 
once. Due to this observation, it is recommended that as many as possible future scenarios is included 
and investigated, as this is assessed to increase the quality of the results.  
 
11.5 Simulation process 
When analysing the developed design solutions, it is crucial to emphasise that not every possible 
combination of the input variables has been simulated. Due to the nature of the Monte Carlo Method 
process as described in Section 6.1, only a certain range of the actual obtainable designs within each 
design category are simulated. As this study seeks to investigate how different design solutions perform 
both structurally and with regards to flexibility, this neglection of combinations are accepted. However, 
when discussing which designs are optimal with regards to the different KPI’s, it should be noted that 
the proposed optimal solutions only represent optimums within simulated designs; other potentially 
optimal solutions might lie within the regions of the unexplored design combinations.  
 
If the study were to be continued with the objective of determining the most optimal solution(s), it could 
be relevant to utilise an optimisation algorithm. Galapagos is one proposal for a component in Rhino 
Grasshopper which facilitates evolutionary solving of a problem with multiple input parameters. 
Nonetheless, utilising such algorithm is considered out-of-scope of this study and no further 
demonstration of such tool is outlined.  
 
To address the objective of optimising multiple parameters simultaneously, the principles of multi-
objective optimization could be introduced. Whilst incorporating this optimisation process is considered 
out-of-scope in this study, a short introduction within its possibilities is given.  
 
An example for utilising this method, could be to optimise the use of material (mass), the sound impact 
insulation level and the deformations all at once. To visualise this concept, a 3-dimensional scatter plot 
is presented in Figure 126. While this plot is outlined with three arbitrary axis and datapoints, its 𝑓1, 𝑓2 
and 𝑓3 axes could demonstrate these three parameters.  
 
 
Figure 126: 3D-scatter of a Pareto frontier for 3 objectives (Ibrahim, Vargas, Deb, & Rahnamayan, 2016) 
 
==PAGE:101==
92 
 
11.6 Investigated KPI’s 
The investigated KPI’s in this study are chosen on behalf of an assessment of their significance to the 
overall structural performance of the developed slab designs. Bending, shear, and rolling shear stresses 
are oftentimes some of the critical stresses when designing CLT in ULS, while deformation often can 
be critical in SLS (Swedish Wood, 2019). Due to the triangular shape and the distribution of forces as 
tension and compression in trusses, these stress types are often critical for such structure and thus 
assessed relevant to include also (T. H. Chu, 2010). The mass of the structure functions as a benchmark 
to compare stresses and deformations between design solutions while simultaneously maintaining an 
objective of decreasing this value. Reducing mass is a very significant step in reducing the CO2-footprint 
of a structure, and without this KPI the performances would be based solely on statics which does not 
reflect a holistic perspective on inventing a new load-bearing structure. Additionally, the impact sound 
insulation is included because of its ability to describe the acoustic feature of a horizontal slab structure.   
 
When analysing stress distributions, it can be debated whether high or low stress values are the most 
optimal.  In conventional structural engineering the utilisation rate is often the guiding design criteria for 
stress checks. It is directly connected to the calculated strength of the investigated performance and its 
related limit value. In most cases the utilisation rate is linked to strength properties provided by 
standards, and in many design cases it serves as the only principle of check. It is employed in both 
SLS, ULS and ALS designs, and can be applied for manifold types of checks such as deformations, 
vibrations, stresses etc.  
 
For this study, low stresses are assumed to reflect a good performance. Yet, it can be debated whether 
a high and low stress value is to be considered optimal for a structure. The argument for approaching 
a high stress value is, that the utilisation rate increases proportionally to the strength. A high utilisation 
rate yields a structure which is highly utilised, thereby avoiding unnecessary use of material. Due to 
obvious reasons the utilisation rate cannot exceed 100%, as this would mean an overrun of the defined 
limit value for the investigated performance. Thus, a closely approach to a utilisation rate of 100% would 
be the objective of the design. However, when incorporating the principles of flexibility into the early 
design process, the effect of a high stress value can suddenly reverse and cause potential future 
trouble. In the extreme case of a structure being fully utilised from its design stage, thus incorporating 
a utilisation rate of 100%, its purpose of use is automatically limited to include its design scenario. 
Future changes which in any way leads to an increase of stresses (or any other check of utilisation), 
would be un-doable as this potentially could lead to a collapse of the structure, unwanted deformation 
magnitudes, or any other undesirable behaviour. From this point of view on stresses and their 
magnitude, and since this study focus on the investigation of flexible timber-composite slabs, stresses 
in this study are assessed to be optimal as their magnitude decreases.  
 
Nonetheless, the discussion on how stress magnitudes are recognised in flexibility context is interesting. 
There is currently no correct answer to this question, and in many cases of measuring flexibility the 
assumption will be project specific, and thus the responsibility of the designer or engineer to decide. In 
this study the relationship between bending, shear and rolling shear stresses are relatively proportional 
due to the formulas of calculating their magnitude as presented in Section 3.3, yet one could imagine 
design scenarios where such relationship does not occur. In this case, a high stress value would be a 
prudent scenario because a future scenario could potentially induce stresses which are desired, as 
these structurally could counter-act other unwanted structural behaviours. In a such case the flexibility 
assumption for the exact KPI would be reversed, and an increase in stress magnitude would be 
considered optimal behaviour.  
 
==PAGE:102==
93 
 
11.7 Neglected KPI’s  
Due to a limitation of time, this study has neglected several structural checks which often are deemed 
mandatory in the verification process of both CLT and the applied truss systems. Apart from the six 
investigated KPI’s of bending, shear, rolling shear, deformation, tension, and compression a range of 
other structural checks which could have been of relevance to include is discussed.  
 
11.7.1 KPI’s related to design of CLT 
Of significant impact on the design process of CLT, calculations of vibrations and natural frequencies 
in SLS should be mentioned. The process of performing such checks is often time-consuming and can 
be advanced (Koppelhuber, Pock, & Wallner-Novak, 2013). Due to the geometric layout of the 
developed slab designs, the connections between the truss members and the CLT panel(s) are 
assessed to some extend function as intermediate supports. This is suggested to potentially provide 
structural rigidity to the cross-section, by reducing the theoretical span length due to the intermediate 
support, which might reduce vibrations induced by footsteps or similar movements. In Equation 11.2 it 
is seen how the length L2 contributes to reducing the eigenfrequency which limit value for residentials 
often are 8 Hz (Petersen, 2020). However, such suggestions are not further investigated in this study 
and is assessed to require advanced Finite Elements Analysis to measure actual eigenfrequencies.  
 
𝑓1 = 𝑘𝑒,1𝑘𝑒,2
𝜋
2𝐿2 √
(𝐸𝐼)𝑥,𝑒𝑓· 106
𝑚𝑔
 
11.2 
 
Of other neglected verifications like the one of eigenfrequencies, calculations of accelerations and 
calculations of stiffness criterions for a single point load of 1 kN at midspan, should be mentioned, as 
these are other significant checks to be conducted in SLS (Petersen, 2020). 
 
Another neglected investigation is of local impact forces on the CLT panels. This phenomenon would 
be particularly relevant to examine at the joints between the CLT and the trusses. Due to occurrence of 
compressive forces carried by the truss members, such forces will have to be transferred onto the CLT 
panels where they will be re-distributed in the timber panel. The compressive forces could be applied 
as point loads acting perpendicular or slanted to the fibre direction of the outer lamellas which is the 
weak direction with regards to compressive strength (Koppelhuber, Pock, & Wallner-Novak, 2013).  
Such point loads could also induce both local shear and rolling shear stresses around the joint which 
also could be of relevance to investigate (Petersen, 2020).  
 
11.7.2 KPI’s related to design of trusses 
The main purpose applying trusses in structures is often to reduce both bending and shear stresses 
and deformations, by carrying external applied forces internally as tensile and compressive forces (T. 
H. Chu, 2010). For this reason, the inclusion of axial tension and compression as KPI’s in both GL and 
CHS profiles has been chosen. Significant neglected calculations on truss members of both GL and 
CHS to emphasise is calculations of lateral torsional buckling (LTB). Buckling behaviour occurs in 
members subjected to either compression or a combination of both compression and bending and is 
often a critical design parameter in structures with slim cross-sections (Porteous & Kermani, 2013). The 
developed design solutions in this study are not assessed to create large bending and shear stresses 
in the trusses, yet such claim would have to be further investigated.   
 
11.8 Joints 
A further significant part of designing a truss structure is to construct and verify the joints between both 
CLT and truss members and internally between trusses. In this study, the joint stiffness has been 
automatically defined through Karamba3D, and all forces and stresses distributed amongst the different 
==PAGE:103==
94 
 
members through connections has been assumed to be acceptable in magnitude. The detailing of joints 
has been neglected and no calculations related to such has been conducted.  An extensive investigation 
of both the design and practical application of the different joints is proposed to be executed, to 
structurally be able to verify the design solutions. To discuss possible solutions of how CLT and either 
GL or CHS profiles could be internally connected, a range of proposals on joint detailing is provided. 
The proposals are presented as an inspiration on applicable solutions and detailed structural properties 
on presented solutions are not included.  
 
    
 
Figure 127: Concealed ALUMIDI brackets from producer Rothoblaas (Rothoblaas, 2023) 
 
The producer Rothoblaas offers several bracket solutions for timber-to-timber connections. One 
example is their bracket ALUMIDI, which is a hight strength aluminium bracket concealed in the 
connected beam, which in this study is the GL truss. While ALUMIDI at current level does not allow for 
connecting slanted elements to a surface, Rothoblaas does offer customised solutions which resemble 
their standard catalogue products but are project specific. A graphical concept proposal on how 
brackets could be customised to connect CLT panels and both GL or CHS trusses are visualised in 
Figure 128.  
 
   
 
Figure 128: Proposals for concepts of a customised brackets connecting CLT and either GL or CHS profiles. 6

Additionally, internal joints between the CLT panels would have to be designed too. A standard practice 
of connecting CLT panels is by introducing screw-connections parallel to the span of the panels. Such 
screw-connections can carry significant shear forces in both vertical and horizontal direction, depending 
on the dimension of fasteners and the internal distance between such.

11.9 Structural fire safety 
Investigation of structural fire safety is one of the core processes of verifying a load-bearing structure 
which is designed for an environment exposed to risks of fire hazard. Such investigation has been 
conducted to calibrate and verify the calculation methods applied in the simulation script as explained 
in Chapter 6, where the baseline designs are investigated for stresses confer methods of executing 
ALS-calculations prescribed by Eurocode. This has been done by applying load combinations and 
theories of charring rates of Cross Laminated Timber (Dansk Standard, 2007).  
 
However, due to the more advanced geometry of the developed design solutions, the investigation of 
structural fire design is neglected in the further analysis of the structural performance in Chapter 9. The 
reason for this is, that Karamba3D does not incorporate components to determine charring rates and 
reduced cross-sections, parameters which are to be considered when performing fire design. 
Furthermore, the behaviour of CHS profiles in exposed to high temperatures can be unpredictable and 
should be a focus point which requires additional fire design detailing.  
 
While neglecting any detailed analysis of the fire resistance of the developed design solutions, it is 
proposed that especially design solutions of Category 1 incorporating both an upper and lower CLT 
panel does provide a significant resistance to fire. According to Danish building regulations, horizontal 
slab structures must be verified for a fire with a duration of 30-120 minutes depending on the related 
fire class (Bolig- og Planstyrelsen, 2023). In KLH®’s ETA-standard a burning rate of 0,65
𝑚𝑚
𝑚𝑖𝑛 is defined 
(European Technical Assessment, 2021).  
 
In Figure 130 a simple plot outlines the reduced cross-sections of four arbitrary CLT panels subjected 
to fire cases of R30, R60, R90 and R120. The fire is in this case assumed to be a nominal, standard 
fire according to ISO 834-1:1999 and delamination of lamellas are neglected (Petersen, 2020). The 
chart clearly shows that panels of a thickness >60mm does have a remaining cross-section after R120, 
which is the most critical fire case. This behaviour shows that design solutions of Category 1 with a 
lower panel of a thickness of at least 70 mm, does protect the upper trusses and top CLT panel for fire 
hazards, thus allowing these elements to sustain their structural integrity. While the load combination 
for ALS do not amplify live loads as outlined in Table 5, Category 1 of design solutions does have 
characteristics to potentially perform well in critical fire situations. Nonetheless, it must be noted that a 
further detailed analysis of stress distributions in ALS is required to verify this hypothesis. A risk of 
accumulation of both high bending, shear and rolling stresses due to the reduced cross-sectional area 
is assessed to potentially occur. This relationship between cross-section area and stress magnitude is 
also expressed in Equation 3.12, 3.14, and 3.19.  
 
==PAGE:105==

11.10 Acoustic properties and mass correlation 
The impact of structural mass on the acoustic insulation value 𝐿𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤 is considered to potentially be 
problematic, particularly for designs with a low mass. As per the Danish building regulations, impact 
sound insulation in horizontal structures between dwellings must be a 𝐿𝑒𝑞,𝑛,0,𝑤≤53 𝑑𝐵. Although the 
baseline scenario of this report depicts an office building, the scenario of a transformation from office 
to residentials is feasible. This correlation is also visualised in Figure 87, where the optimal solutions 
with regards to mass performs the worst in insulation for impact sound.  
 
One solution to overcome this potential issue is to add extra weight for impact sound insulation in the 
flooring build-up. Since 𝐿𝑒𝑞,0,𝑤 is reliant on the mass of the floor structure, materials such as gravel, 
sand, crushed concrete, og similar high-density substances can provide the necessary permanent 
mass. Such alteration will also positively contribute to the mass 𝑚𝑔 resisting vibrations in the slab, as 
also outlined in Equation 11.2.  
 
Important factors to keep in mind when adding extra permanent load to the flooring are the stresses 
and strains in the structure. For the baseline scenario a permanent load of 1
𝑘𝑁
𝑚2 has been included to 
represent any relatively light-weight arbitrary flooring-build up. However, if a layer of e.g., 80 mm of 
gravel with a density of 16,5
𝑘𝑁
𝑚3 were to be added, this would increase the permanent dead load with 
approximately 1,5 kN. Such intensification in load could potentially require larger dimensions in the 
design process.  
 
Yet, one could argue in this case whether such load already has been included in the load combinations. 
Due to strict and non-dynamic code prescriptions, structures are to be designed for live-load 
requirements which potentially never reach a magnitude anticipated by code. In such case a structure 
is verified for predicted yet imaginary loads, instead of responding to actual subjected load instances 
for this specific structure.  
 
To reflect on such a suggestion, an example scenario like the baseline is considered. Two types of 
loads are present: live load and permanent load which subjects a total characteristic sum of 5

11.11 Designs with intermediate support 
Due to a limitation of time for this study, only design solutions adhering to the future scenario of FS-1A 
are analysed and discussed. Yet, it is noteworthy that every design category has been simulated for 
designs which also adheres to the future scenario FS-1B. FS-1B depicts a future addition of an 
intermediate support at midspan. This scenario could represent a supplement of a load-bearing wall or 
beam, introduced for structural or aesthetic reasons and its static influence can be great as outlined in 
Figure 57. 
 
A scenario like this is considered relatively likely to occur, and thus relevant to include in a flexibility 
analysis of a load-bearing slab.  Even though it is neglected in this analysis, the simulated KPI’s for all 
design solutions and combinations of scenarios can be accessed in Appendix 3 (Digital). Calculated 
results can be utilised in the examination of how the developed design solutions respond to such 
structural alteration, however, is not further analysed.  
 
11.12 Internal ducting 
In Section 6.4.2 it is described how a future scenario could involve the introduction of internal ducting 
in the slab structure. Such a change could potentially include an increase in requirements of air inflow, 
thus also an increase in required ducting volume. Due to a limitation of time, not all developed design 
solutions have been investigated for a such scenario. However, two randomly selected designs are 
chosen, to outline and visualise why the possibility of integration of ducting in a slab structure is deemed 
a flexible design feature.   
 
Two different examples visualising how internal ducting for HVAC etc., can reduce the slab height is 
presented. Each example showcases two scenarios: one where ducting is routed parallel to the slab 
direction and one perpendicular to the span. The duct dimension applied for reference is Ø300. The 
measured slab height excludes further flooring build-ups, suspended ceilings etc. For the example, two 
random design solutions utilising GL for trusses are chosen.  
 
==PAGE:107==
98 
 
11.12.1 
Semi-integration of HVAC 
The first example outlined can semi-integrate an Ø300 duct in the direction parallel to the span, while 
the ducting must be routed beneath the cross-section of the slab perpendicular to the span direction. 
The possibility of a semi-integration provides this slab the future ability of adopting a Ø300 HVAC-
ducting system in the direction parallel to the span, by only reducing the floor-to-ceiling heigh 
insignificantly.

11.12.2 
Integration of HVAC 
Second example can fully integrate an Ø300 duct in the direction both parallel and perpendicular to the 
span direction. This integration of ducting saves 720𝑚𝑚−570 𝑚𝑚= 150 𝑚𝑚 of slab height. In a 10-
storey building this could potentially save a total of 1500mm in height, which could be distributed and 
added to the floor-to-ceiling height or reduce the total costs of the project.  
Table 43: Total slab height of example 2 
Slab height - Parallel to span direction 
Slab height - Perpendicular to span direction 
550mm 
570 mm

Figure 135: Placement of ducting perpendicular to the span direction. Side- and front view. 
 
 
==PAGE:109==
