13. Recommendations for a step-by-step guide on 
measuring flexibility of a load-bearing structure 
In this chapter a step-by-step guide to measure the flexibility of a load-bearing structure is proposed. 
The guide is established as a universal guide to measure the flexibility of various load-bearing 
structures. Thus, it is not restricted to slab structures only. The guide should be treated as instructive, 
and its individual steps and their sequence are not deemed mandatory. Due to the composition of this 
study, the individual steps have been executed in the different chapters of the report. 
 
1. Define one or multiple load-bearing structures which are to be measured for their flexibility. This 
could be any member contributing to carrying vertical loads or acting as horizontal bracing etc. 
 
2. Define a baseline scenario. The baseline scenario represents the traditional design approach 
and function as a benchmark for the assessment of future scenarios influence on the load-
bearing structure (Carlucci, Fioritio, & Negendahl, 2023). A strong point of origin for defining 
the baseline, is to consider a common practice scenario used for a conventional verification of 
such structure. For this application, proposed design criteria provided by Eurocodes or similar 
standards may be applied.  
 
In this study the baseline scenario exhibits a common design scenario for an office building. 
This includes an evenly distributed live load of 2,5
𝑘𝑁
𝑚2, which indeed applies as the standard 
practice live load scenario for office buildings (Energistyrelsen, 2013).  
 
3. Secondly, Key Performance Indicators (KPI) are to be defined. When designing load-bearing 
structures manifold different verifications are often executed. Mostly Eurocodes and similar 
standards are used as a guideline to perform such checks, which often are within both ULS, 
SLS and ALS. The selection of KPI’s is based on a subjective assessment by the designer. 
Since verification checks vary between different structures, the chosen KPI’s will often do so.  
 
KPI’s acts as a benchmark for assessing flexibility. It is essential that the KPI’s describes a 
value which is somehow affected by the change of future scenarios. When measuring flexibility 
of a load-bearing structure suggested KPI’s for selection are stresses, forces, strains, 
vibrations, or similar structural properties which describes certain performances of the 
investigated structure. Furthermore, one could include KPI’s such as mass, costs, or CO2-
footprint. These are KPI’s which necessarily are not affected by the future scenarios, yet they 
provide a basis for comparison between designs also.  
 
This study includes eight KPI’s in its measurement of flexibility. These are chosen due to their 
significant impact on the design process of CLT panels and trusses.  
 
4. Next step is to determine which future scenarios could influence the performance (KPI’s) of the 
structure. In this assessment, one must consider the relative impact of the scenario on the 
investigated design. A strong tool for investigating such is the Impact and Probability Matrix 
introduced by K. Negendahl, F. Carlucci and F. Fiorito. This method places future scenarios in 
four different regions, based on their relative impact on the KPI’s and their probability of change. 
Again, this assessment of scenarios and their relative impact and probability of change is the 
responsibility of the designer. While no further method of this assessment is proposed in this 
==PAGE:110==
101 
 
study, it is recommended to base solutions on quantifiable data. An example could be in the 
assessment of live load requirements due to a typology change. In such case, it would be 
relevant to examine the percentage of transformations of office buildings into other uses in that 
specific context. By doing this, one could evaluate the chances of which future live loads could 
be of relevance to consider. 
 
Two future scenarios have been selected in this study: change in live load magnitude and live 
load distribution. Both scenarios are judged to be of general high relevance to consider when 
selecting future scenarios for load-bearing structures.  
 
 
 
Figure 136: Impact and Probability Matrix (Carlucci, Fioritio, & Negendahl, 2023) 
 
5. In this step the investigated structural member is subjected to both the baseline and the future 
scenarios. Depending on the number of combined scenarios and chosen KPI’s (and 
investigated structural members) a parametric calculation tool is recommended to employ in 
case of multiple calculations or simulations etc.  
 
6. When selected KPI results have been determined for every possible combination of design 
solutions subjected to both the baseline scenario and future scenarios, the analysis of such 
results can be conducted. The analysis provides the basis for measuring the flexibility of the 
investigated structure. Three different methods are proposed in this study: 
 
Method 1: Calculate 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖 using Equation 3.2 for every KPI result. 
 
 
𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖= 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝑆𝑖−𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝐶𝑆
𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝐶𝑆
 
 
13.1 
Plot 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖 as boxplot. If multiple designs are assessed, one can compare individually between 
such. Apply the Classification system as presented in Figure 4 to provide the design with a 
classification.  
 
Method 2: Calculate 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑛𝐶𝑠 and 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑛𝑆𝑖 using Equation 3.3 and 3.4. This method preserves the 
unit of the investigated KPI. 
 
 
𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑛𝐶𝑆= 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝐶𝑆−𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝐶𝑆 
13.2 
 
==PAGE:111==
102 
 
 
𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑛𝑆𝑖= 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝑆𝑖−𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑘𝐶𝑆 
13.3 
 
Plot 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑛𝑆𝑖 as boxplot. If multiple designs are assessed, one can compare individually between 
such.  
 
Method 3: This method is effective if the objective is to measure the flexibility and compare the 
results across multiple designs. For the investigated KPI, determine the median of the 
investigated and the IQR: 
 
𝐼𝑄𝑅= 𝐼𝑄𝑅3 −𝐼𝑄𝑅1 
13.4 
 
Plot the results for all datapoints in a scatterplot and compare between the designs. For most 
KPI’s a low median and low IQR is desired, as this induces the ability to obtain the changes. 
However, this assumption is not always true and must be assessed by the designer.  
 
It is recommended that a combination of either Method 1 and Method 3 or Method 2 and Method 
3 is performed, when multiple designs are investigated for their flexibility.

developed design solutions. The highlighted designs are purely chosen due to their aesthetics and 
appearance. Moreover, DS2.9 - 14 and DS2.11 are presented, as these were the most optimal

the designs are selected to showcase the design concepts.

15. Conclusion 
From the outlined theoretical framework, a range of composite slab structures has been developed by 
combining Cross Laminated Timber (CLT) and Glued Laminated Timber (GL) or steel Circular Hollow 
Sections (CHS) in various compositions, utilising a parametric script to perform structural calculations. 
Based on their performance, a spectrum of designs has been selected for further analysis of their 
structural behaviour and their flexibility. The conducted analyses form the basis of a designation of 
optimal design solutions, both structurally and with regards to their flexibility. Moreover, the results of 
the analyses contribute to the proposal for a step-by-step guide on measuring flexibility of a load bearing 
structure.  
 
The theoretical framework was outlined to examine and review state-of-the-art theory, research, and 
literature on flexibility in the construction industry, innovative slab structures, and CLT. The theory on 
flexibility highlighted several different approaches for assessing the different layers of a building, such 
as the Shearing Layer Theory and the Open Building Theory, each dividing a building into layers of 
different purposes and lifespan.  
From the conducted review of structural calculations of CLT, a parametric script was constructed in 
Rhino Grasshopper and Karamba3D, to perform simulations of different designs. Since CLT is not 
included in the member-library of Karamba3D, a calibration of the script was executed to ensure 
accepted stress and strain distributions of the model. The calibration procedure was performed by 
defining a range of load scenarios for two KLH ® CLT panel types. Structural calculations of stresses 
in both Ultimate Limit State (ULS) and Accidental Limit State (ALS) as well as deformations in 
Serviceability Limit State (SLS) were conducted for both panels. For determining such results, the 2D-
software KLH Bemessung and the Finite Element Analysis (FEA) software Strusoft FEM-design were 
applied to assure semi-accurate stress and strain distributions of the developed Karamba3d script. A 
review of the results across the software showed minor discrepancies in stresses and strains of the 
developed script and the other software, however such inconsistencies were accepted.  
Based on references of trusses in bridge engineering and the reviewed innovative slab structures, a 
range of geometric concepts of different composite slabs were proposed, to perform a basis for the 
development of designs. A parametric application of the concepts in Rhino Grasshopper, combined 
with a Monte Carlo method for randomisation were applied to construct and simulate a total of 46000 
unique composite slab designs.  
To provide a foundation for the analyses of the simulated designs, a range of future scenarios assessed 
to be of both high impact on the structure and of high probability of change, were introduced by applying 
the Impact and probability matrix. Moreover, a baseline scenario for a slab structure applied in an office 
building was created. Selected scenarios included live load magnitude, load distribution, change of 
static system, and integration of internal ducting. Due to limitations of the study, the two first mentioned 
scenarios endured most of the subsequent analytical focus. In addition to the baseline scenario, a 
baseline design has been established. This design depicts a KLH® 200mm 5s TL CLT panel, which is 
assessed to portray a standard CLT slab solution to solve the baseline scenario. The purpose of the 
baseline design is to provide a benchmark for the structural analysis of the composite slabs, thus 
excluded in the flexibility analysis. 
Eight Key Performance Indicators (KPI) have been defined to administer the analyses of structural 
performances and the flexibility of the designs. On behalf of an assessment of their relevance for 
inclusion, designated KPI’s includes various stresses, vertical deformations, sum of mass, and the 
normalised impact sound pressure levels. KPI’s reflecting a performance affected by the defined future 
scenarios are supplied with a limit value sourced from either Eurocode, Danish National Annex to the 
Eurocode, or from KLH ® Massivholz ETA-06/0138.  
Through a three-step elimination process the number of design solutions has been reduced from 46000 
to approximately 450. This was executed by discarding designs yielding KPI results exceeding their 
==PAGE:117==
108 
 
limit value, thus exhibiting what is assessed to be a poor structural performance. To ensure an equal 
comparison methodology, all designs were subjected to the same nine combinations of the future 
scenarios.   
Two individual analyses have been conducted. The first examines the structural performance of the 450 
remaining design solutions after the elimination rounds. Developed designs are investigated and 
compared across the defined KPI’s, where the mass serves as a benchmark for comparing the 
investigated design solutions. Plots visualising the relationship between the mass and either bending 
stress, shear stress, rolling shear stress, tensile stresses, compression, and deformation. From the 
conducted analyses, a clear tendency amongst the different categories of design solutions were visible: 
most design outperform the baseline design across all KPI’s. Moreover, it was observed that DS2.9 (GL 
truss) and DS2.11 (CHS truss), both originating from the same geometric concept are dominating the 
Pareto optimal frontier in most charts. Both designs solution categories adopt a 3D-truss system, with 
no lower CLT panel in its composition, a feature which reduces their mass compared to the designs 
with both upper and lower panels included. Amongst all analysed plots, two specific designs occur on 
all of them: DS2.9 - 14 and DS2.11 - 12. These designs can be concluded to retain the best KPI 
performances, while utilising the least amount of material (CLT and either GL or CHS) to maintain their 
structural integrity. Additionally, DS1.1 and DS1.3 dominates the lower regions of both bending, shear, 
and rolling shear stresses and deformation charts yet cannot be concluded to be optimal as their 
exploitation of mass generally is higher compared to DS2.9 and DS2.11. However, if the objective were 
to reduce such stresses or deformations DS1.1 and DS1.3 would provide the best design to solve this.  
The flexibility analysis was conducted utilising three distinct methods each proposed in the theoretical 
framework. These methods are inspired by a technique originally developed for measuring the flexibility 
of a building component with regards to energy consumption of a building. The flexibility analysis 
incorporates three different methods for measuring the flexibility of the developed design solutions. The 
first method involves deriving 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑉𝑘𝑖, a unit-less value which is normalised against both a baseline and 
a range of future scenarios. The second method derives 𝐾𝑃𝐼𝑛𝑆𝑖, and considers identical scenarios, yet 
incorporates the unit associated with the investigated KPI. Lastly, a method for measuring the flexibility 
of multiple designs at once were proposed, which plots the median and IQR for investigated KPI’s.  
Due to the large number of designs, the three best performing designs determined in the structural 
analysis was selected for a measurement and comparison their flexibility. Moreover, the baseline design 
was included in this subset to serve as a benchmark for comparison. The results of this analysis showed 
that all selected designs can be classified for a Class 3 according to the Flexibility Classification System. 
Only the baseline design is situated just between Class 3 and Class 4, thus the developed composite 
slab designs are concluded to be slightly more flexible with regards to rolling shear stresses. 
Furthermore, the analyses using the Median/IQR-plotting method demonstrated a nearly linear 
relationship between the median and the IQR across all designs and KPI’s. Moreover, this investigation 
of the relationship revealed that the flexible regions with a low median and IQR primarily were 
dominated by DS1.1 across the KPI’s. Yet, DS2.9, DS2.11, and DS1.3 also exhibited a variety of 
designs which fell within the more flexible regions of the Median/IQR-charts.  
The objective of this report was to propose, test, and review a method for measuring the flexibility of a 
load-bearing structure. This goal has been achieved by conducting analyses of the developed designs. 
Yet, this method of measuring flexibility is not concluded to be finalised. Due to urgent needs for more 
sustainable building practices, the research on how to create, design, and measure flexible buildings 
should continue to achieve even more sustainable buildings, able to adapt to a rapidly changing future.
